0061537934|1.0|The only reason I got past the first few pages of this sentimental wallow was that it was our book club choice (curses be upon she who chose it!)  After I'd waded through to the excruciatingly twee ending, I checked the reviews, and was struck by the fact that I could find not one single published review by a known critic - only 'customer reviews', the vast majority uncritical.  This alone confirmed by belief that this is not a book to be taken seriously.  Unfortunately it was not even a book I could enjoy.  Others have noted the turgid prose, the emotional manipulation, and the shallow characterization. I have a new grouse; one of the things this dog must have watched on TV (well. someone must have slipped in a DVD) was 'The Secret', as he apparently swallowed whole its inane belief that anyone can have whatever they want simply by wishing for it fervently enough.  This is denoted in the book by the oft-repeated meaningless mantra 'That which you manifest is before you'.  At one point (p.124) the dog narrator strongly implies that if only Eve had had the right, positive attitude, she need not have died of cancer.  Silly girl.  (If anyone is interested in statistics that blow this particularly pernicious 'blame the victim' philosophy out of the water, read 'Bright Sided' by Barbara Ehrenreich.) Once I'd spotted the association with 'The Secret', I realized that it permeated the book, and it became more and more repellent.  The ending, when (spoiler alert) the dog's wish to become human is granted, symbolizes the ultimate triumph of this idiotic worldview.  You have only to wish, and your desires will be granted!  Yes, I know it's just fiction.  But I can't help but believe that Garth Stein was using it as a vehicle to push what are presumably his own beliefs.  And I don't like them one bit.

B004LWZW4C|5.0|The latest edition of X-Men films, \\"X-Men: First Class,\\" is inspired by the 2006-2007 comic that showcased the beginnings of its most famous members beginning with Professor X, Angel, Beast, Cyclops, Iceman, and Jean Grey. However, in this film, the team roster includes Professor X, Magneto, Beast, Mystique, Banshee, Havok, Darwin, Sebastian Shaw, Emma Frost, Azazel, Riptide, and Angel (no, not that Angel - Angel Salvadore a.k.a. Tempest in the comics). Do not let the huge cast fool you, though; this film belongs to Charles Xavier/ Professor X (James McAvoy), and Erik Lensherr/Magneto (Michael Fassbender)! Although not accurate to all of the origins in the comic, \\"First Class\\" still successfully captures what makes the X-Men so great (and this coming from a guy who prefers DC Comics)! It does not take a philosophy major to tell you that the X-Men are an allegory for race relations, and this film - taking place first during the Nazis' cruel rule and genocide over the Jews in 1944, and then during the turbulent times of 1962 when race relations were at their most volatile - perfectly executes this allegory with its James Bond-type style, solid story and near-faultless character development.<br /><br />  My first question when asking wondering about this film was, \\"Is it canon with the first three X-Men films?\\" The answer is a definite \\"yes\\" - especially with two particular surprise cameos in the film. I have to admit that I was a bit skeptical when the movie started because of the recycling of young Lensherr's first display of his magnetic powers (as seen in Bryan Singer's 2000 film [[ASIN:B001PPGAJE X-Men [Blu-ray]]]), but, fortunately, that is just the beginning of his - and the other mutants' - journey. I haven't been a big X-Men comic fan so I wasn't disappointed by the discrepancies of character origins. For those of you who are fans of the comic, trust me, the origins are greatly altered. One of the great additions to this film was Kevin Bacon as main villain Sebastian Shaw. Bacon has been in his share of hits as well as stinkers, and in most of those films, he's played a good guy. This time, his role proves two things. First, he can act, which is something most critics could tell you. Second, he should play a bad guy more often! Bacon's Shaw is a true villain whose mutant power makes him seem almost indestructible, and therefore, makes him a truly formidable foe. His inevitable defeat is carefully crafted and I think very well-executed. On the other hand, while January Jones' Emma Frost fits the physical traits of the character, her mercenary-like attitude and complex personality are not displayed so well. Many critics have panned Jones' performance, but I also blame the writers who merely didn't give her many good lines - or a history, for that matter - to work with. Still, who knows? Hopefully Frost's history will be explored in the inevitable sequel.<br /><br />One of the best aspects to this film is the relationship between Lensherr/Magneto and Xavier/Prof. X. One of the most powerful, well-acted scenes in the film is when Xavier is trying to teach Lensherr how to hone his magnetic power. Whereas Lensherr had merely used his anger to unleash his power, Xavier teaches him that finding the balance of anger and serenity can help intensify his power, making it possible to lift heavier objects. When Lensherr's memory is revealed to Xavier and the audience, the bond between McAvoy and Fassbender is both poignant and an inspired piece of acting; it captures the \\"bromance\\" relationship between the two in a touching way. And that's what this film is based in: the deep relationships between the characters. Their love, their fear, their anger; all of these emotions are what drive the characters in their actions as well as their relationships (i.e., Magneto and Prof. X; Mystique and Beast; Shaw and Magneto), and the writers (and director Matthew Vaughn) stylishly pulled it off. Bryan Singer tried to achieve this same tone in his2000 debut of the franchise. However, while the original \\"X-Men\\" was good, it was not as good as this. I think it is because of the focus of characters. In the original, the main focus was on Rogue and Wolverine, and in this, the focus is on Professor X and Magneto. While Rogue and Wolverine have interesting backgrounds - particularly Wolverine who is practically immortal because of his regenerative healing - the two characters were not written well. There were so many aspects of their histories that could have been explored but were not (fortunately, Wolverine later got his own origin movie). In \\"First Class,\\" X and Magneto were focused on just right while also introducing the other assorted characters. There's something I love when the audience is given a glimpse into how a once-good person turns evil. These kinds of stories remind the viewer that anyone could go down this route because most people have a past that is not always pretty; and Lensherr's past is particularly horrifying. \\"First Class\\" simply gets it right!<br /><br />  I have heard that this is the beginning of a new film trilogy and I surely hope so because this is by far the best of the franchise to date. I'm going to say that this is the best comic adaptation of the year! It balances the drama, action, humor and romance in all the right ways. Plus, let's face it; the explanation of Xavier's paralysis is so much better in this film than in the comic. This is one of those films that will be a hard act to follow, but it's finally a proper film for a great franchise. Now, if they could only get my two favorites - Daredevil and The Punisher - right! That would be uncanny.ebut of the franchise. However, while the original \\"X-Men\\" was good, it was not as good as this. I think it is because of the focus of characters. B000AYEL6U|5.0|North & South is a tale of people shaped by a time and place. There is first Margaret Hale, the compassionate, strong willed daughter of a country churchman; Mr. Hale, the aforementioned former pastor who, moved by doubt and conscience, resigns his post in the rural south of England and moves his family to the bleak, northern industrial town of Milton; John Thornton, stern-faced master of a cotton mill who, driven by family tragedy, strives to maximize the ever important bottom line without destroying his workers in the process -- all for the sake of the business, of course; Mrs. Thornton, John's mother, who is as fierce an advocate for her son as she is a ruthless capitalist; Nicholas, the determined union boss who sees the need -- and opportunity -- for improvement; his daughter, Bess, who has paid the ultimate price for the industrial revolution; etc.<br /><br />This is very much a human story, a story of people separated by a vast chasm of varied experience and situation, who must, through bitter trial and even at times against their will, come to recognize at least a modicum of virtue in their fellow man's opposing viewpoint. Thus, as it begins, to Mr. Thornton the smoker who endangers the entire mill and its hundreds of workers by smoking amidst highly flammable conditions, is a threat who must be dealt with expeditiously and fiercely; to Miss Hale, he is an unarmed man who daren't defend himself, and the beating of him is an exercise in excess and cruelty. To Nicholas and his fellow mill workers, the master's claim that there is no money to meet their demands is proof of duplicity and further abuse; and, to Mr. Thornton, it is the reality of his world. There are at work in this film several competing factors -- the generous nature of slow, easy-going rural life contrasted with the cold realities of a fast, hard industrial world; the bottom line for a business as opposed to the comfort and welfare of its employees; even the moderate climate of the south versus the chill of the north.<br /><br />As these many, competing themes come into play, friendships are formed, lives are destroyed, hearts are broken, and life goes on as it always has. This is a story of people in the midst of turmoil. It is bleak, terrible and hopeless at points; and for all that it probably ends on too optimistic a note to ring wholly true. But that is not a bad thing -- after portraying so much suffering and pain, it is a relief to have a happy ending (or as happy as one can expect, given the circumstances).<br /><br />This is a phenomenal series; the story is, as you may have guessed from my description thus far, agonizingly good, but the screenplay, acting, costumes and settings are all perfect. The two main stars are phenomenal, but so too are the supporting characters; everyone becomes who he or she is supposed to be. 5/5 stars -- a great story perfectly executedll of the north. I am currently re-watching all 19 episodes (I have the older version of the boxset without all the bells and whistles) and it is not only bringing me back, but it is making realize just how smart this show really is.  Because, not only the writing for the teenagers, but also the conflicts between Patty and Graham are so accurately portrayed that it gives me the shivers.  I have been married for several years and been through a lot of ups and downs in my marriage.  I can say in all sincereity that Patty and Graham's relationship is incredibly true and honest. This is a new aspect that of course I was not aware of when I watched the show live on ABC. I suspect that the reason why this show was cancelled was more to do with how uncomfortable it made parents feel about thier own marriages and lives, coming to terms with growing older, etc.  I doubt it was due to the drama with Angela and her friends as much as everyone thinks.       This is such a great depiction of a real marriage (well, a real, troubled marriage, I guess)  Unlike Angela and co., who analyzed everything ad nauseum, Patty and Graham tend to gloss over their feelings and instincts...they are able to distract themselves with work and the kids and sweep the problems with their marriage under therug. They stuggle to find happiness as adults approaching middle age, both within themselves, as a family, and within the marriage.  They seek out their true identities and realize that they have lost little pieces of themselves over the years, without even realizing it.  While I am a good decade younger than the characters' ages on the show, I completely relate to them and how they are presented.      In all, this is a timeless show no matter what the viewers' age.  As long as humans are watching it will never be obsolete.

B004RPUGAS|3.0|The setting is 1935 Missippi in a small town divided by race. A trial pits justice against bias. A black man stands falsely accused of raping a white woman with a dubious reputation- there is no evidence except her testimony backed by that of her idle father.<br /><br />Tom Robinson stands to lose his life if the conviction stands. Atticus Finch is a lawyer motivated by justice despite the culture of bigotry he grew up in.<br /><br />Atticus must fight the state's case which is backed by no physical evidence or corroborative testimony. Tom Robinson's good character seems to count little in the eyes of the town as the dubious character of the alleged victim's father is overlooked. The father, Tom Ewell, testifies and is quickly discredited by Atticus.<br /><br />Despite the conviction of Tom Robinson, Tom Ewell, the victim's father is down in the dumps...quite literally since that is where he lives...in the city dump...white trash perhaps in a very real sense. The angry Ewell seeks revenge on the kindly Atticus.<br /><br />Perhaps predictably, the black man is killed in a fusillade of bullets as he tries to escape a work farm pending an appeal which may have succeeded.<br /><br />MOCKING BIRD- in an apparent nod to Faulkner- has a dark id force residing in the house next to Atticus'. Boo Radley is the son who disappeared twenty five years before as the apparent victim of a repressive religious upbringing that stresses fundamentalist pietism.<br /><br />Boo communicates throughout the book with furtive glances from his window and gifts left for Atticus' children. A ghost of a man is Boo when reality should have made him a sociopath.<br /><br />MOCKING BIRD is narrated from the point of view of Scout who apparently learned to read at four on the litigation weary lap of her father when most children have bedtime stories. The real author of both books was as much the Zeitgeist of 1960 as Steinbeck or Lee. A real account with a fiction like reality to it. MOCKING is fiction that seems fictional indeed.<br /><br />In MOCKING BIRD we find a lawyer strangely removed from the culture he grew up in and unmoved by the passions of ego, angst or the lust created by loneliness. A kind of secular saint who turns his cheek to all and answers only with wise sayings and dry wit.<br /><br />Atticus is no Huey Long being ruthless to the ruthless in the name of the working class. Atticus seems strangely eastern in the dark land of Faulkner and decayed houses...he is no sweating redneck messiah as Govenor Long was but an abstraction with no real faults except perhaps his idolatry of the law.<br /><br />Atticus is a hollow icon incapable of true good because there is no struggle with evil in him...Atticus is programmed for justice.<br /><br />Atticus' South is only a New England with humidity...its soul is hollow because it lacks thecreaking floorboards of dark carnality. There is not enough rot in this book to make it great; it can only preach to us from a pulpit or as a footnote to TV documentaries on Civil Rights.<br /><br />MOCKING BIRD cannot see into the dark souls behind the white hoods.<br /><br />Would the issue of justice had been less if Tom Robinson had been a poor white falsely accused of raping a wealthy white woman or a communist union organizer murdered in the cotton fields of the anti-labor South? B0012GN47C|5.0|Somehow I'll never quite understand why Carlos Santana took such a pounding from critics and his audience following this first trio of albums. It's true he did begin making a series of albums that explored his jazzier leanings with people such as John McLaughlin,Alice Coltrane and Flora Purim. Than again it wasn't as if albums such as [[ASIN:B0000062FL Abraxas]] didn't have strong jazz elements built into them. For a man whose guitar style resembles few outside of John Coltrane's sax style it's important to note that a base of funk and jazz were always at the core of Santana's sound. That Carlos himself elected to expand on that as the 70's progressed said a lot for his musical integrity. Even when the \\"psychedelic stew\\" that Santana was originally mixed into had gradually faded as the 70's soldiered on. But jazz-funk was happening and that's what Santana by and large chose to embrace here. Bringing in Leon Ndugu Chancler on drums and keyboardist Tom Coster from that particular world we were reminded that Santana's albums were generally released in trilogies. Or groups of three similarly themed albums coming right after another. And if Carlos had anything to do with it funk would be a big part of his mid/late 70's trilogy of which this was a part of.<br /><br />  \\"Dance Sister Dance\\" is about as catchy a beginning as one could ask. Even more strong involved with the salsa-rock style than anything on the first Santana albums and in many ways more pop oriented it did let people know what they were getting. Because Santana's music usually always comes out of jazzy improvisational jamming \\"Take Me With You\\" and the Mexican folk/jazz-rock fusion hybrid of \\"Gitano\\" were good for the band because it put them into sync more with the musicians than popular tastes,making sure they'd have some timelessness. Also the funk the present here is of different varities and that's important too. \\"Let Me\\" is very uptempo,filled with lightening speed clavinets,vocal harmonies and percussion. Actually my own favorite number here is the one a lot of people seem to despise. \\"Tell Me Are You Tired\\" is elegantly produced sophistifunk-pop friendly,multi faceted and with very clean rhythmic lines. On \\"Europa (Earth's Cry Heavens Smile\\" the focus is on Carlos and his playing and,as usual he really takes his technique of making the few notes he plays really wiggle and wobble to the next level on this beautifully melodic piece. The album concludes with \\"Let It Shine\\"-funk in all it's splendor with phat synth lines,JB style rhythm guitar,tons of wah wah and a great gospel like chorus.<br /><br />  That Santana elected to rejuvenate his sound by embracing the musically expansive genre of funk as opposed to making some type of progressive rock album (as a lot of psychedelic acts tended to do at this point) said a lot about how much of a jazz ethic Carlos had. Not only on his own but by association. It's kind of too bad that,even though this was by and large a commercial success it didn't exactly signal the beginning of any hugely successful era for him either. The trilogy this album began didn't exactly progress as successfully as his first and resulted in a similar holding pattern by the end of the decade. At least on the commercial side anyway. Musically with each release,Santana was expanding musically and spiritually. And while everyone was welcomed and encouraged to come along on this creative journey,the musical divisions created as funk began to extend into the disco era begin to derail the runaway fright train Santana's music was starting to become. But no matter how you cut it this represents Santana at their strongest and is one of their highest quality and vital albums following their original trilogy.avinets,vocal harmonies and percussion. The album concludes with \\"Let It Shine\\"-funk in all it's splendor with phat synth lines,JB style rhythm guitar,tons of wah wah and a great gospel like chorus. Two books ago, this series could do no wrong.  Times have sure changed and some degree of disillusionment has been going on.  What the heck happened?<br /><br />People have come into this series with a great deal of high expectations, a lot of it fairly warranted.  By most accounts and while this is by no means a consensus, the first three books in the series are really, really awesome.  They were a fantastic mix of fantasy and sheer operatic grit, operating in a world where the rules weren't much different than here, even if the stakes were higher.  Wedded to a fascinating back story and given a huge cast of characters who were torn between scheming to get on top and sheer survival, you had a whole confluence of events that were clearly racing toward a conclusion that wasn't going to be pretty but was going to be worth the ride.  Then Martin had to split up the fourth novel into two parts (the second of which is this novel, as well as parts of the next one) even as the books themselves got longer and the gaps between them became longer as well.  What had been taking only two years in between books was now taking up to five, and while four years got us three books, it's taken a decade to get two more.  None of that should matter if the novels are good, though, right?  It's the ultimate quality that is the deciding factor, right?<br /><br />Unfortunately by lengthening the gestation process for each book he's only raising expectations higher.  We're wired that way, go figure.  While we still expect greatness because we're unfair and unforgiving that way, by putting five years in between each book it's almost implicitly promising that it's going to be mind-blowing and completely worth the wait.  And is that what we get here?  Not quite.  So, what's happened?  Or what is happening?<br /><br />As it says on the package, this book picks up on all the stuff that we didn't get to see in Book Four, notably what everyone's favorite dwarf Tyrion and future dragon-queen Daenerys are up to, as well as a host of other characters that were involved in that part of the planet.  Splitting the books up, while it probably made sense from a publishing perspective, has really only served to dull the momentum of the books.  While the early novels were masterpieces of intensity, here it feels like the pace has slackened somewhat and seems to be taking a more leisurely trot toward the bloody conclusion we all know is coming.  Tyrion is a case in point . . . he's constantly referring to events that happened at the end of the third book, which for him was a very recent event.  For the rest of us, it was close to a decade ago and feels off for some reason.  We've been apart from these people for too long and they seem to have changed somewhat in the interim.  Or maybe we have.  Everyone goes through their motions and furthers their plans but it all seems somehow more . . . static.<br /><br />This is the big problem with the book and seems to be a fairly common complaint.  It promises the awesome without actually bringing it.  He had his pick of fairly interesting plotlines to work from and further.  Maybe too many and that may be another reason that the novels feel somewhat blunted.  With so much to work with there's no way to keep all those balls dancing in the air without the pace suffering somewhat.  He's got a hard task in front of him . . . having set all these plots in motion and scattered them to the four winds he's got to bring them all back so that the players are all on stage for the conclusion, a task right now that probably feels like steering the Titanic.  Which may explain why a good portion of this book feels like it's treading water, marking time waiting for all the elements to interlock and come together.  Tyrion spends the entire book trying to get to Daenerys, without really succeeding.  In times past he would have met up with her before the book hit the halfway mark and livened up the scene with an alliance of scheming.  Here everyone is like blind people feeling their way around the same room.  Everyone is close, but nobody knows it and thus they never interact.  Daenerys, waiting for Tyrion, can't do much and so falls in love and kills time with courtly matters.  Then does something fairly important and vanishes for what feels like hundreds of pages.  Oops.<br /><br />The other chunk of the book is taken up by Jon Snow and his merry crew over at the Wall.  The Night Watch has been waiting for winter it seems since the book started and the rumored presence of the Others has been haunting the background of events almost since the beginning.  But again, it feels like we're stuck in a holding patterns, with the Others j-u-s-t about to attack and for a thousand pages never really making an appearance.  Again, maybe it needs to all happen at once, but until then he sort of dithers and comes to no firm decisions, making preparations for stuff that isn't going to happen yet.  He argues with the Watch, he deals with the Wildings, he argues with hardcore hopeful king Stannis and at the end of the book we're almost where we've begun.  Well, almost.  And this is the other part of the problem.<br /><br />We get a thousand pages of everyone moving in circles and then right toward the end the awesome starts to come back as events begin to speed up again.  Stannis makes a move that might go poorly, Jon Snow gets a move made, Daenerys puts herself in a different position and Tyrion could potentially make everyone's life miserable (which has been consistent at least) . . . and then the book ends.  We get all this setup, with very little of the consequences and Martins leaves us on the cusp of events being about to explode.  Which on some level is fine, if the next book contains all these explosions then people will probably proclaim as regaining that vibe that has been missing all this time.  And when the series is done and capable of being read as a piece people probably won't even notice how this book seems transitional.  But we need explosions here now because we're reading these novels as they come out and since we're in some way financing the next book or two (or improvements on his house, or his stamp collection, it hardly matters) we're at least owed some nod to the serial format.  Asking for a book that operates as a complete book on its own and also as part of a larger piece is not too much to ask for, especially when years go by between novels.<br /><br />Ultimately, none of this may matter.  This may indeed be a transitional piece, necessary because the huge amount of plots made splitting the last book necessary.  If he can follow through on what barely gets started here, wrap it up in two books (plausible, the elements for the finale are getting into place) and get them out at a reasonable pace (even if his and my definitions of \\"reasonable\\" probably differ), it will be seen as a slightly flawed masterpiece with a great beginning and end and a slightly bloated middle.  That's okay.  Getting all the characters back together in the same novel is a step in the right direction.  Everyone is too scattered here, and that intensity that informed the early books because everyone was constantly scheming and playing off each other is missing here.  We need interactions between the main host again.  We need them to start plotting directly to kill each other.  Everyone right now is inhabiting their own separate short story inside the novel and we need them to be part of one novel again, racing toward the conclusion.  It's possible, it really is.<br /><br />Martin's storytelling skills are intact, at no point did I want to fling this across the room.  It kept me reading for a thousand pages.  That's no mean feat.  Some sequences are close to fantastic, all the stuff with Reek, Arya's chapters, the brief glimpses of Jaime and Cersei.  Tyrion remains entertaining and once Daenerys becomes a mover and shaker (plus one new character who the jury is still out on) things could happen rapidly.  The potential is there.  I care about what's there and what's missing.  I want to know what happens with Bran, with Brielle, with Catelyn Tully and the Vale.  I love the flashbacks we get and how Aerys and Rheagar and all the people we never saw feel like real characters even though we've only heard about them through the voices of others, how it feels like myth real people witnessed.  I love how the history breaths and is constantly being made before us.  I know he's under a lot of pressure, even if he'd maybe never admit it (maybe he's not, he is a professional after all).  You can only hear the \\"modern Tolkien\\" comparisons for so long before you start to realize that people are expecting a masterpiece for the new century, when all you wanted to do was tell this nifty fantasy story.  But we read and we're here and for the most part, we're sticking around until the end.  So, just relax and tell the story and let it unfold.  It doesn't need to be perfect and huge and epic and all those things that high fantasy is supposed to be, just good.<br /><br />Ultimately this will only be seen as a blip.  You've got all the ingredients in front of you and two more books.  You've got this, George.f in a different position and Tyrion could potentially make everyone's life miserable (which has been consistent at least) . . . and then the book ends.  We get all this setup, with very little of the consequences and Martins leaves us on the cusp of events being about to explode. At the risk of incurring the oh so \\"insightful\\" disses of my positive review from the trolling trolls going through and accusing the writers of robo-reviewing or of having been somehow monetarily compensated by GRRM for their having attested to liking his book, let me just say I loved this one. B00005JLF5|5.0|Star Trek: Deep Space Nine was launched midway through the 1992-93 viewing season.  Living in a household where The Next Generation was a fixture, I found the concept of a Star Trek series which took place at a fixed location - as Mr. Spock would put it - fascinating.  My teenage mind ascertained the idea that instead of the characters being on a ship travelling to the action, the action came to them.  Some viewers disliked that idea, but I didn't.  In fact, I came to enjoy Deep Space Nine more than I'd enjoyed Next Generation.<br /><br />Much of the series' premise was established during the pilot episode, \\"Emissary.\\"  Starfleet Commander Benjamin Sisko (Avery Brooks) has been assigned as the Federation commander of Deep Space Nine, an assignment he protests.  He's not alone; his first officer is Bajoran major Kira Nerys (Nana Visitor), a fiery woman lamenting the Federation's presence in her home, fresh out of a 50-year occupation by the Cardassians.  After a meeting with Kai Opaka, the Bajoran spiritual leader, Sisko learns he is the emissary to their prophets and must locate the Celestial Temple.  This he does, discovering a stable wormhole to the Gamma Quadrant in the process.  After an internal struggle over his career choices and to finally come to terms with his wife's death, Sisko decides to stay on Deep Space Nine.<br /><br />This first season contains several firsts, including the debut appearances by Garak a Cardassian working officially as a tailor whom Dr. Bashir (Alexander Siddig) suspects to be a spy, Grand Nagus Zek, the leader of the Ferengi alliance and Bajoran spiritual leader Winn Adami (Louise Fletcher) who consistently uses traditions and literalism to bolster her position, as well as her adversary, Vedek Bariel Antos.  In addition, there are numerous Next Generation crossovers.  For starters, Chief Miles O'Brien (Colm Meaney) was the only frequent Next Generation character to become a regular on DS9.  According to the pilot, Sisko's command instructions are delivered on board the Enterprise by Jean-Luc Piccard, who'd been assimilated by the Borg and present at Wolf 359 where Sisko's wife was killed.  Plus, the Klingon sisters Duras, Q, Vash and Ambassador Lwoxanna Troi all put in appearances during this season (Ambassador Troi would appear twice more in subsequent series).<br /><br />When this show was new, it was easy as a viewer to relate with many of the individual character arcs.  Sisko struggles to accept his assignment as station commander while trying to be a good father to his teenage son, Jake (Cirroc Lofton); Kira tolerates serving jointly with the Federation; Dr. Bashir deals with his self-absorption as a medical \\"wiz kid\\" and his nagging attraction to Lt. Jadzia Dax (Terry Farrell); security chief Odo (Rene Auberjonois) makes no bones about his dislike of Quark (Armin Shimmerman), the Ferengi bartender; Chief O'Brien looks for ways to make the station feel like home for his wife, Kaiko and daughter, Molly.  It's easy to sympathize with all of the regulars, especially if you've been forced to live and work someplace you disliked.  The comforting part is, by the end of this first season, all the co-inhabitants have learned to work together and depend on each other when serious problems arise.<br /><br />Of all the series regulars, the one I was most fascinated by was Dax, a character from a species known as Trill.  Though the Trill were touched upon during Next Generation, few members of that race were included in Next Generation or DS9, and it was essentially through Dax that the viewers learned about the species.  Jadzia's antecedent host, Curzon Dax had been present during the events concluding Star Trek VI: the Undiscovered Country (helping map out a peace treaty with the Klingons). He was an aficionado of Klingon culture and of Ferengi gaming (especially tongo).  He was a functioning alcoholic with an interest in women which would get him in trouble on multiple occasions.  In his elder years, Benjamin Sisko was Curzon's prot&eacute;g&eacute;, a trusted confidant and the best friend he ever had.  Curzon Dax died two years before the Federation arrived at Deep Space Nine, when Sisko met Jadzia Dax for the first time.  Their friendship would continue and provide a source of drama, comedy and suspense.protg, a trusted confidant and the best friend he ever had. 0553801473|5.0|I promised myself I would not review anything that already had more than 100 reviews; only a megalomaniac would bother throwing his two cents into an overflowing piggy bank. Those lords who have not been hurt by the war become disproportionately powerful, allowing some secondary families to become important players.<br /><br />Much of the book explores the wisdom of our familiar characters in unfamilar situations: will Tyrion's wits save him, now that he is no longer a rich Imp? Tyrion ,Jon, and Dany all act rashly: in Dany's case, more often than not. My favorite characters do stupid things and it annoys me.<br /><br />I am sympathetic to all of these complaints. B0058H8V6K|4.0|Natalia Kills, labelmate of fellow pop perfectionists Lady Gaga and Robyn, has made quite the name for herself overseas, and with any luck, she'll hit it big here in the United States as well. Perfectionist, her debut album, is, quite simply, one of my favorites to be released this year. Because she does manage to showcase different styles of contemporary pop, I believe a song by song review is appropriate:    Wonderland: A dark pop song, definitely one of the best on the album. Natalia does a good job singing the song and the lyrics are definitely one of the highlights of the cd. 5/5  Free: This is about as cute as she gets. It's fun to sing along to, but overall it's one of the more disposable tracks. The will.i.am feature on the American edition seems superfluous to me, and I prefer the original. 3.5/5  Break You Hard: Another highlight of the album - Natalia strikes a nice balance between dark and fun with this one. One of my faves! 4.5/5  Zombie: A slower song about her \\"zombie\\" lover. Not one of my favorites, but the lyrics are neat and it definitely showcases the darker side of her music well. 2.5/5  Love Is A Suicide: The most uptempo song on the cd is great too. The beat is killer and gregorian chants are always cool (though who would've thought to combine the two?). 4.5/5  Mirrors: The lead single is also one of the most immediately striking and notable songs on Perfectionist. Just a generally all around awesome song. I could see this definitely having been a hit if it had been released by someone more known. 5/5.  Acid Annie: My least favorite song from the album. Coming off the high point of \\"Mirrors\\" it doesn't measure up to it in virtually all respects. Any of the bonus tracks from the digital deluxe or international edition would've been a better selection. The lyrics are cool, though, but that's about all I like from this track. 2/5  Superficial: Another slick uptempo that's reminiscient of \\"Free\\". It's a decent song, and defintely not the worst on the album, but it gets overshadowed. 3.5/5  Broke: Another one of my favorites! Natalia takes the time to slow down the tempo. The lyrics are a bit cliche, but her earnest delivery and the solid beat make this one of my favorites. 4.5/5  If I Was God: This is another stellar song as well. The lyrics are relatable and smart, the production is nearly flawless, and the vocals are spot on in both emotional delivery and pitch. Definitely worthy of the album closing position. 5/5    Total Average: 4/5    Overall, this is definitely one of my favorite albums to be released this year. However, the bonus tracks on the digital deluxe edition are definitely worthy of downloading. These include \\"Perfection\\" (a spoken introduction to the album; nothing especially notable but it does help to add flavor), \\"Heaven\\" (a sad downtempo about a deceased loved one; like Broke, her delivery makes this song, and it's almost criminal to leave this out in favor of \\"Acid Annie\\"), Kill My Boyfriend (a cutesey song made notable only by the juxtaposition between the cheery instrumental and the dark lyrics), Not In Love (another dark track, similar to but slightly better than Zombie), and Nothing Lasts Forever (my last favorite, an uptempo slightly like Superficial but with really great production and great vocals). To me, with these songs included, Perfectionist is absolutely a 5/5 album, but the standard edition sacrifices nuance in favor of a more streamlined listening experience. I would recommend buying the deluxe edition, as I think it's 2011 pop at it's best (It brings the great club beats without being either mind-numbingly shallow or heavy handed with faux-deep messages and lyrics), but if you're trying to save a couple of bucks or simply prefer a physical copy, I still wholeheartedley recommend Perfectionist.

0062060554|4.0|Article first published as Book Review:Before I Go To Sleep by S.J. Watson on Blogcritics.<br /><br />Waking up daily, not knowing who you are or where you are can be quite frightening. When the man in bed next to you is also a stranger, fear begins to take over.<br /><br />In Before I Go To Sleep by S. J. Watson, we follow the lives of Christine and Ben as they try to work through the horror of amnesia. Christine's amnesia is much like that Drew Barrymore experienced in the movie titled 50 First Dates. However, in Christine and Ben's life the humor is gone. Every day Christine follows the same path, asks the same questions, and looks at the same pictures, hoping that something will strike that spark she needs to gain her life back. She does not remember anything past her 29th birthday, including her marriage and husband. The explanation she receives is that her amnesia stems from and accident, and as she asks further she is only more concerned.<br /><br />Each day she retains what she has learned, but each morning she wakes up to the very same confusion. Ben leaves the house the same time each day, while Christine attempts to put her life together. A call from a young Dr. Nash, changes her life. At her meeting with him, she actually finds she has been secretly visiting him to help her find the answers. One of his recommendations is that she writes in a journal daily, everything she does and anything she remembers of her prior life. He agrees to call her daily once her husband has left the house to remind her to look in her diary. As she continues on this mind-bending journey, each day as she receives her call to look at the journal, she is always surprised at the notation on the first page. She cannot remember what it means. 'Do not trust Ben.' What could that possibly mean?<br /><br />As the journal becomes her lifeline to the world, she finds bits of scattered memory returning. She finds that her loving husband has been lying to her, not only was her injury caused in a more horrifying way than he described, but she has a son as well. When asked about her best friend, his response was to assure her that she had moved away and that they had lost contact. This too appears to be a lie. Why is this happening, Christine tries to find her own answers, yet she must begin at the very start each day. Danger seems to hang in the air. Is that oppressive doom coming from Ben, her husband of 22 years, or is there more about Dr. Nash then meets the eye.<br /><br />Watson has done an admirable job at creating the hopelessness of feeling that comes to Christine as an amnesiac. Each day starts over with fear and concern, and even as she follows her clues to prepare her daily ritual, she is obsessed with finding out more. Almost afraid to sleep each night knowing what the next day will bring, she begins to rely on her journal, writing in it obsessively. It is her life and her memories and there is fear of the unknown should it disappear. Her husband is still in the dark about her visits with Dr. Nash, and her small bits of recurring memories. She is not sure why, he is so patient and loving with her, yet she struggles to trust him.<br /><br />The terror slowly builds as you immerse yourself in the hopelessness of the situation, and you feel both the pain and joys experienced as Christine begins to find pieces of her life. The story sucks you in for a roller coaster of emotion, and Watson keeps you guessing as to the suspense that is building. Each step drags you further into the morass, urging Christine forward and yet warning her away. The dialogue takes you into her heart and mind, creating a relationship that seems personal. Can Christine find the answers before the danger strikes?<br /><br />If you enjoy a good suspense, this book delivers. It is a quick paced and depth filled look at both hopelessness and dreams. The characters are well-written and quite charismatic. Following Christine, even with her slight despondency, she never stopstrying to find the answers. You have to admire her tenacity, and she leaves a lasting impression.<br /><br />I would recommend the book for a reading club. Christine's life is both interesting and informative. The discussion could create a great deal of dialogue within a group. Before I Go To Sleep would be a great addition to your library, one of those stories that you could read repeatedly and still find information you may have missed.<br /><br />This book was received free through the authors publicist. All opinions are my own based off my reading and understanding of the material.acity, and she leaves a lasting impression.     I would recommend the book for a reading club. Christine's life is both interesting and informative. The discussion could create a great deal of dialogue within a group. Before I Go To Sleep would be a great addition to your library, one of those stories that you could read repeatedly and still find information you may have missed. 0316098337|4.0|This is one of those books I just couldn't put down; I read it in one night. Having not read any reviews before I purchased the book, I was unsure what to anticipate and half expected to quickly become annoyed by a novel written in the voice of a small child. However, I was pleasantly surprised. Emma did an outstanding job of capturing the family dynamics between mom and boy while cleverly allowing the reader to experience the ordeal through the innocence of a child -- who illustrates his small world as though he's drawing a picture of a loved one. Had the book been written in any other point-of-view, it would've lost its charm. I found the first half of the book to be the most appealing, as the room quickly became one of my favorite \\"characters\\" in the story -- because Jack loved it so much. Once we lost the room, it was like losing a piece of him. But the loss was for a good cause. The ending was strong, and left me wanting more. All in all, I very much enjoyed the book and found it to be one of the more original works I've read this year. I'm looking forward to reading more of Emma's writing.

0553381695|4.0|This book is much, much better than its predecessor, mainly because there is a huge improvement in the pacing. Looking back, Game of Thrones is definitely about setting up arranging all the pieces to be in position for the action that follows in the sequel. While that seemed tedious at times, Clash of Kings definitely makes it worth the effort to get through. Twists and turns continue as the people of Westros play the Game of Thrones for control of the Seven Kingdoms. What strikes me the most (in a good way) about Kings is the way that Martin manages to deftly juggle subplot upon subplot. While the lords of the Seven Kingdoms jockey for power in an all-out war, North of the Wall the Black Brothers are tracking down a mystery that may change the face of the civil war in the south. Meanwhile, the East, across the Narrow Sea, the last scion of the former King is on a magical and exotic journey through a land that seems bizarre to say the least compared to Westros, a place where magic is born again and dangers lurk around every corner. Martin does a fantastic job at selecting characters for points of view of the action on all sides and each chapter ends on a tantalizing note that makes you want to continue forward. Again, the pacing is really good and just when you're about to find out what happens to Bran, we're whisked away to the other end of the world to find out what's happening with Daenerys. In spite of how that sounded, it's not annoying, and the title of each chapter brings all the action that was left off the last time we saw that character rushing back, making you eager to continue. Martin is politically savvy, does intrigue really, really well and manages to build likable, but extremely round characters. There are no fantasy archetypes here. They're buried under layers and layers of flaws that make some of the characters endearing, and others utterly repulsive.<br /><br />So why not 5 stars? Every. Single. One. I get it. It's period-accurate and all that jazz, but you convinced me of that in the first book and through other aspects of this one. I don't need to know that Cersei is sitting down to a seven course meal with a three page long description of each of the courses as if I'm perusing the menu at a fine dining restaurant only to be confronted with the meal that Tyrion is eating in the middle of the next chapter. Were you hungry when you wrote this? Seems like it. Anyway, the descriptions just became tedious and I found myself skimming whenever Martin started to mention food. Two books in and I also still can't get used to the very uncomfortable descriptions of rape that seem remarkably casual given the gravity of the act. Again, I understand the whole Alan Moore of Fantasy effect he's going for, but at times, it seems tasteless and with all of Westros at war looting and pillaging neighbors, there were lots of moments that I felt were unnecessarily graphic. B002PMV9FG|5.0|I took a break from the Harry Potter movies after I saw the fourth, which I believe was one of the worst movies I have seen in some time. I find the book itself to be perhaps the weakest of the series (at least in its contribution to the seven-book narrative), and the film adaptation seemed more interested in simply realizing as many scenes in the book as possible rather than creating a coherent story.     After seeing the Deathly Hallows movies, however, I decided to view movies five and six to see if David Yates did as brilliant a job as he did with book seven. He did. With Order of the Phoenix and Half-Blood Prince he was not afforded the luxury of a two-part release, but these films are nevertheless excellent films.     Fans of the series complain that Yates acted as a rather poor surgeon on the book, using a meat cleaver to ineptly amputate scenes from Rowling's book. These fans miss the point. The Harry Potter movies are designed as summer blockbusters, not as realizations of the books. The movies must merely capture the essence of the book, and present the plot as coherently as possible. I believe Yates to be the only Potter director to understand this. When this concept is understood, I think disappointed fans will find that many scenes they thought they lost actually ARE in the film. I will give but one example:    There is no opening scene between the two ministers.     True, but what, really, was the point of this scene? As a Muggle, the Prime Minister offered those readers not obsessed with everything Potter to get a nice crash course in all things Wizard. In a film, we really do not have time to re-explain what has already been covered in five films. The other point of this scene was to show how Voldemort's rising power affects Muggles and Wizards alike. Do we really need Rowling's clunky dialogue between two men in an office to show this? Film is a visual medium, and the collapse of the pedestrian bridge over the Thames brilliantly depicts the terror growing in the non-Wizarding world.     If you're looking for the books, go to your bookshelf and read them. Half-Blood Prince is an excellent movie that not only captures the spirit of the book, but is also re-watchable. Highly recommended.

B000TGJ80S|5.0|4.5 stars out of 5 (plus a half-star for a fantastic Collector's Edition DVD)<br /><br />Dracula is Bram Stoker's classic tale of gothic horror and romance which is considered one of the definitive works in English literature.  It really put vampires on the map as one of the world's most well-known monsters, with the infamous Count of the book's title becoming the most famous vampire of all time.  In film Dracula has become the most used villain of all time, appearing in an estimated 217 films over the last 100 years, and has been portrayed by screen legends that include Bela Legosi, Christopher Lee, and Frank Langella.  The only character with more appearances on film is detective Sherlock Holmes (233 films).<br /><br />Despite his high number of screen appearances very few films have actually attempted an accurate reconstruction of Stoker's novel.  That is until legendary director Francis Ford Coppola came along in 1992.  The legendary director who had brought the world The Godfather, and Apocalypse Now has a rough spot during the 1980s and was determined to make a comeback by bringing Dracula to the screen in a way that focused more on the concepts of gothic romance than horror.  Even more shocking, he decided he would do this only utilizing the same special effects wizardry that would have been available to the pioneers of cinema back in the years Bram Stoker wrote his landmark novel.<br /><br />Bram Stoker's Dracula was finally released in 1992 is at its heart an art film first, a story second.  Sure, it is to date the most accurate adaptation of Dracula ever committed to film, but as narrative it does have flaws.  It makes up for these flaws with Francis Ford Coppola's incredible, uncompromising vision of the notorious bloodsucker.  Is it a perfect adaptation of Stoker's work?  Not really, but for the most part it succeeds at separating itself from the countless other adaptations.<br /><br />Count Dracula is without a doubt the most well-known villain in English literature.  Everyone knows who and what Dracula is.  With such a famous character of both literature and film, Coppola had to think hard about the casting of Dracula, and he succeeded with his choice of British actor Gary Oldman.  Oldman was, and remains to this day the chameleon of Hollywood.  Give him a role, a background, and an accent and he will dissolve into just about any role and he does just that with his portrayal of Dracula.<br /><br />Most of the film naturally follows the well-known story of Dracula's arrival in London to begin corrupting and feeding off Mina Harker (Winona Ryder) and the ill-fated Lucy Westenra (Sadie Frost).  What separates this from previous adaptations is Coppola's direction, which aside from taking artistic liberties, puts a much greater emphasis on the sexual aspects of Stoker's novel.<br /><br />Oldman's Dracula is a far more tragic character than previous incarnations.  Coppola's film takes liberties in giving Dracula an origin story; providing reasons for why he is the way he is.  A gorgeously shot opening reveals that Dracula (as his real-life inspiration Vlad III the Impaler)  he was a devoted man of God, viciously slaughtering men to protect the church.  But when his beloved takes her own life and is thus denied entry into eternal salvation Dracula raises his sword against the cross, renouncing God and swearing to live off the blood of others.  This entire sequence, highlighted by Wojciech Kilar's fantastic score provides one of the film's best moments, and also helps separate it from the countless other attempts at adapting Dracula to the screen.<br /><br />Special note does need to be given to Wojciech Kilar's score, which was sadly not nominated for an Oscar.  Lots of his themes really drive home this movie's imagery.  His score radiates a feeling of dread and evil, yet still manages to give off that feeling of seduction that is so inherent of Dracula.  At times, especially in the prologue, I can even hear bits that seem to be borrowed by Howard Shore in his soundtrack for The Lord of the Rings trilogy.  I am very proud to own this film's soundtrack and listen to it on a frequent basis.<br /><br />Production is top of the line with Michael Ballhaus' cinematography mixed with the on-screen (or \\"nave\\") special effects helping to create a film that both feels new and old at the same time.  Throughout the film you'll constantly be asking \\"How on earth did they do that?\\" or \\"Oh, I see what they did there!  Awesome!\\"  The real magic of Bram Stoker's Dracula rests in its visual trickery.  If you don't have any other reason to see this adaptation, give it a watch just to see what the crew was able to accomplish without digital effects.<br /><br />But, sadly the film is not perfect, and I would be called out if I were not to mention its flaws.  Aside from Oldman, and perhaps Anthony Hopkins, the acting is very unbalanced.  Winona Ryder is fairly bland as Mina, as is Sadie Frost as Lucy.  Even Hopkins as Van Helsing at times seems to be phoning it in, but he does work in the role because, if for no other reason, he's Anthony friggin Hopkins.<br /><br />The crown jewel of the films weakness comes in the form of Keeanu Reeves in what is easily the film's worst casting decision (perhaps even the worst performance of Reeves' career...).  I know it's popular to beat on Reeves wooden acting, and I don't want to seem like I'm jumping on the band wagon but: it's Keeanu Reeves as a turn of the century British gentlemen, complete with a cheap, unbelievable accent.  I'm sorry, but this really couldn't work and I wonder what good old Coppola was thinking.<br /><br />Also, aside from some of the acting flaws, towards the end of the film the narrative begins to become a tad bit jumbled, as if they started running out of time to shoot more transition scenes.  It isn't obvious, or confusing, but the pacing does seem to take off on a sprint once Dracula begins his seduction of Mina.<br /><br />So what is my final verdict?  While Coppola's Dracula certainly have its flaws it does manage to make up for them with its striking imagery and by digging into the sexual aspects of Dracula that other films had shied away from.  It is a flawed masterpiece to be sure, but for someone with as visual a mind as me it is hard not to love the film for what it is.<br /><br />The Collector's Edition includes an introduction and commentary by director Francis Ford Coppola, but the second disc provides the juiciest features with over an hour's worth of featurettes exploring the creation of the film.  Given the non-existent features of the single-disc release I highly suggest investing in the Collector's Edition if you have the intent of adding this film to your collection.undtrack for The Lord of the Rings trilogy.  I am very proud to own this film's soundtrack and listen to it on a frequent basis.      Production is top of the line with Michael Ballhaus' cinematography mixed with the on-screen (or \\"nave\\") special effects helping to create a film that both feels new and old at the same time.  Throughout the film you'll constantly be asking \\"How on earth did they do that?\\" or \\"Oh, I see what they did there!  Awesome!\\"  The real magic of Bram Stoker's Dracula rests in its visual trickery.  If you don't have any other reason to see this adaptation, give it a watch just to see what the crew was able to accomplish without digital effects. B0053Y9AXQ|3.0|These eight albums and various singles, all recorded between 1957 and 1960 represent the transition of Cooke from gospel star to one of the greatest soul singers of all time.  The songs range from jazz standards and broadway show-tunes to then current pop hits, and they are about the most mellow performances of Cooke's career.  The material on the albums represented has been available off and on through various RCA budget and repackaging offerings over the years, though initially most of it was issued on the Keen label.  The digital transfers are definitely from LP, and while in some cases they're a bit wanting, for the most part they're acceptable, especially considering the source and age of the recordings.  If you're looking for the hits, go to the ABKCO packages.  If you want the great gospel tracks, go to the Specialty packages.  But if you want some great late night Cooke, this is the place to find it.  The albums in the package are: Sam Cooke (1957), Encore (1958), Tribute to the Lady (1959), Hit Kit (1959), I Thank God (EP - 1960), Cooke's Tour (1960), Hit's of the '50's (1960), Swing Low (1960) and various singles released during the same period that were not included on these albums.  Unfortunately there are no liner notes or album cover reproductions, and the remastering is only okay, so it's three instead of four or five stars.  Maybe RCA or ABKCO will eventually find the time to do this package properly with remastering from the original tapes and thoughtful notes and cover reproductions, but in the meantime, this will have to suffice.

In my experiences lifting weights I have found that there is a since of personal satisfaction you get from being able to lift a certain about out weight.  By just using my own body weight I do not get that same satisfaction.  For example, the satisfaction I receive from being able to bench press 3 plates (315 lbs) without a spot helping me was a great personal high for me.  Being able to do 5 or 6 one handed push-ups does not achieve the same sense of satisfaction. With the exercises that use just body weight I have discovered that at times as I begin to fatigue myexercise form declines.  Therefore, even though I have done more repetitions of the excise than I could before I cannot take the same sense of satisfaction for it because I know my form was not correct.      Even with the above issues I have I would still recommend this book to anyone looking for a detailed workout plan that does not have easy access to a complex gym.  You will get results from this book and you will not have to spend a lot of money or time.  However, if you live next to a gym and have free membership or are rich and can afford to buy all the equipment in the world you might want to look for a book that will help you take advantage of that equipment.

B0007GFFVQ|5.0|Music for Films is probably Brian Eno's second best ambient album behind Another Green World.  This is because you can actually take it two ways- you can enjoy it from a melody stand point *or* as background music.  These two albums give you an option which I personally find *very* appealing because most ambient musicians aren't capable of accomplishing both.    The only difference between this and Another Green World is that... well again, there's two differences.  One is that there's no vocal melodies in sight.  It's strictly instrumental.  Two, there's not nearly as much diversity this time around which explains why I can't quite give in and lay down a 5-star rating for Music for Films.    That doesn't mean the music on here won't fill your head with all kinds of wild, adventurous scenarios.  It will.  My favorite track is probably \\"Slow Water\\".  If you don't actually know this song, it's not as boring as the song title implies.  It's a slow-moving instrumental piece that almost sounds like a piano ballad of sorts, but it's melodic and just unusually pleasant.      The same kind of imagery applies with \\"Alternative 3\\" except this song is MUCH creepier.  The piano is one noticeable aspect, but it's the other exceptionally bizarre arrangements that strike me as downright frightening.  These two songs are my favorites.  Oh, and \\"Two Rapid Formations\\" is totally unique thanks to the way the electric guitar is played.     My gut feeling tells me that most people would consider \\"M386\\" the best song here.  I can see why- it's immediately melodic and contains a creepy vibe overall (not to mention would probably fit in perfectly with Another Green World) however it's a bit too repetitive and predictable for me to give it the highlight treatment.       Overall, you MUST listen to Another Green World first to hear Brian Eno's best example of melodic ambience, but please, make Music for Films your next choice.  You won't regret it.
B00007LTIB|5.0|Yes' seventh studio album Relayer was released in December of 1974.<br />By the summer of 1974, Yes had to deal with some discontentment in the ranks as keyboard wizard Rick Wakeman left the band due to the musical direction the band went on with Tales From Topographic Oceans. At that point, the remaining members of Yes (which comprised of singer/songwriter Jon Anderson, bass player Chris Squire, guitarist Steve Howe and drummer Alan White) hired Swiss keyboard maestro Patrick Moraz (who would join The Moody Blues in late 1978) to record an album that was seen as Son Of Close To The Edge (ironically ex-drummer Bill Bruford left Yes feeling that they would wind up recording Son of Close To the Edge and wasn't his cup of tea) but this was Yes veering into almost jazz-rock territory with great results as I found out in the Spring of 1997 when I bought the 1994 remastered CD.<br />We kick off with the side-long 22 minute epic called \\"The Gates Of Delirium\\". This was lyrically based on Tolstoy's War and Peace. The first eight minutes sees the pre-war gathering and tells of the soldiers going off to war and features stellar musicianship from the band. Then all of a sudden at over 8 minutes is \\"the war sequence\\" begins and was something that was mapped out in Anderson's head before the song was fleshed out and recorded by the band. This part featured screaming synths from Moraz, manic guitar solos from Howe. Plus the rhythm section of Squire and White going off. The sound effects being banged in the war section was White, Squire and Anderson beating on salvaged auto parts snagged from a junkyard. Then the final battle between synth and then Howe's screaming steel guitar. After the smoke is cleared comes the achingly beautiful \\"Soon\\" section to end the piece (which always brings a tear to my eye when I hear it). The piece ends with optimism and hope and the music is beautiful and fades away.<br />The second half of the album starts with the nearly 10 minute \\"Sound Chaser\\" which is a stellar jazz-fusion number that gave  King Crimson, Weather Report and Mahavishu Orchestra a run for their money with some wild lyrics and music playing but an excellent piece (even Alan White said this is one of his favorite Yes pieces). The middle section of this epic features an extended Howe guitar solo accompanied by Moraz's String Synthesizers and laid-back verse from Anderson before going into the wild outro with steel guitar, wild chanting and a synthesizer solo from Moraz. We close this excellent album with another lengthy piece out of \\"To Be Over\\" which is almost country-esque in sound (in thanks no part to Howe's steel guitar) and as laid back as Yes got with their music up to that point.<br />Relayer would reach #5 in the US in 1975 and (like the albums before this) went Gold upon its release.<br />A remastered version was released in 1994 but in 2003 another remaster was released, this time with bonus tracks out of the single edits of \\"Soon\\" and \\"Sound Chaser\\" plus an early take of \\"The Gates of Delirium\\" so you would hear how this epic was fleshed out.<br />RECOMMENDED!hich is a stellar jazz-fusion number that gave  King Crimson, Weather Report and Mahavishu Orchestra a run for their money with some wild lyrics and music playing but an excellent piece (even Alan White said this is one of his favorite Yes pieces). B002MQYOFW|4.0|I will eschew the recap of the story (hundreds of other reviews can do that for you) in order to give you my review from a more literary perspective.  Don't get me wrong based on the title of my review: The Hunger Games is a very entertaining read, I barely put it down, and I immediately purchased Catching Fire.<br /><br />However, with every page I turned, I couldn't help but think of and compare this book to other books or stories, especially Shirley Jackson's \\"The Lottery,\\" Orwell's 1984 and the Richard Bachmann/Stephen King stories, \\"The Long Walk\\" and \\"The Running Man\\" (the original story, not the movie adaptation).<br /><br />Like 1984, there's a dystopian setting pitting the proletariat classes of the 12 districts against the totalitarian elite of the Capitol.<br /><br />Like \\"The Lottery,\\" the Hunger Games tributes are selected at random as a sort of societal penance.<br /><br />Like the \\"Long Walk\\" you get to know the contestants (even the unlikable ones) a little bit, build a relationship of sorts with them as they undergo their grueling trials, even as you know that it can't end well.<br /><br />And perhaps the most fitting comparison is to \\"The Running Man\\" where the protagonist is pitted against hunters and the elements in a fight for survival where the prize for the last one standing is a lifetime of ease and comfort.<br /><br />There's even a little bit of Fahrenheit 451 - a glimmer of hope for the triumph of the indomitable human spirit over brutal fascism.  If I thought about it some more, there are probably other elements cribbed from other novels and authors - though no specific example comes to mind, the whole book reminded me hauntingly of the stories of Philip K. Dick.  Lord of the Flies also leaps to mind.<br /><br />Obviously, if you've never read any of these stories, you're not aware of how The Hunger Games borrows from these themes.  That's not to say that Suzanne Collins has plagiarized any of these works - after all, truly original themes are small in number, and even the greatest works of classical literature have leaned on themes that had been explored by others before them.  In this case, it's a lot like music - sometimes in the process of making an original tune, you arrange some notes in the same order that someone else has in the past.  It's not intentionally copying (you may not even have heard the other song before to know that they sound alike), but as you listen to the song you've created, there are little pieces where you subconsciously catch pieces of other things you sort of recognize but maybe can't quite place.<br /><br />The book was very enjoyable, written in a straightforward first-person prose that reflects the character and setting - simple but not childish, and also without the kind of pretentiousness that you might expect from this kind of protagonist in other novels.  The story is told with a voice that you easily equate with the character.  If I had one complaint, I think the setup for the actual Hunger Games, including the selection and training of the tributes, dragged on a bit too long so that the Game itself seemed very compressed and rushed by comparison.<br /><br />For what it's worth, the Hunger Games Trilogy bundle for Kindle is a more cost-effective option, saving you a few dollars compared to buying all three volumes of the series one at a time.  If I had realized that going in, I probably would have bought the Trilogy - I just saw a few different recommendations for the book recently and was looking for something new to read and picked it up to give it a try.assical literature have leaned on themes that had been explored by others before them. 0307463745|4.0|Rework is a book about how to rethink business-how to rethink your job, how to rethink your colleagues, how to rethink success. So what's wrong with business in the first place?<br /><br />Well, like how many pursuits go awry in life, the emphasis of image over substance (like in Mastery) is the killer. So how does this come across in business? The desire to have a cool business card is one way, the desire to get big fast is another, the desire to use `bizspeak' words when plain English will do is another. There is this temptation to very quickly overcome your small size and limited experience, with a veneer of cheesy tacked on `professionalism,' which really stinks because, of course, everyone around you can smell it. It's a turn-off. And if you're being dishonest about your size, your success and your persona, is it possible you're being dishonest about your products too? Of course.<br /><br />And so this book is actually an antidote for that reckless egotism: rather than emphasize methods to grow big, strong, rich and famous fast (see 99% of Business Books next time your at the Airport), it pursues great products, and emphasizes the varied advantages of actually being small, nimble, and unknown. Instead of fighting against yourself, or chasing a ghost called \\"credibility\\", you use your newness and small size to your advantage. Furthermore, by adopting this `alternate' route to success, your customers, suppliers, partners and fans, can fall in love with the real you, instead of that guy from Wall Street (or worse, American Psycho).<br /><br />There is a great expression, often used in the tech industry: Eating your own dogfood. If you eat your own dogfood, it means you actually use the products your company makes-all the time. There is a fundamental difference here between great companies, and good profitable businesses: some of the best companies in the world were started just because the founders wanted to buy their own product but couldn't find it on the market. Nike started this way, as did Apple, as did 37signals. What's the advantage here? The advantage is a keen awareness (sometimes neurotic is a better word here) and insane obsession with perfectionism. Steve Jobs has famously barked at his designers because the original iPod took too many clicks to get the music to come on. He's not tapped in to what the consumers want, and famously abhors focus groups. What Jobs wants isn't an awesome music player/phone/laptop for you, but for himself.<br /><br />Why doesn't every company do this? Because frankly some companies are just set up to make money. There's a hole in the market,there's an arbitrage opportunity, and there's money to be made. But you don't get fans for being interested in arbitrage. And without fans, you don't get the free advertising. You don't get recommendations. And you sure don't get people carving your company logo into the back of their head.<br /><br />More reviews like this at 21tigeras did Apple, as did 37signals. What's the advantage here? B002ZFQD0E|5.0|\\"The ArchAndroid\\" is essentially a conglomeration of genres (folk, soul, disco, pop, jazz, etc) laced with a sci-fi theme detailing the story of an android named Cindi Mayweather. In a sequel to \\"Metropolis\\" (the first Suite), Cindi fulfills her destiny on the planet Metropolis set in the year 3005 (according to lyrics from \\"Locked Inside\\"), a throwback to Michael Jackson's \\"Off the Wall\\" days. Apparently, the character is meant to be an archetypal medium between the minority and the majority 'uniting the mind and the hand'. In a lovely twist of irony our heroine Cindi Mayweather, the android, becomes the proverbial 'heart' of the piece. If even after a few listens you can't decipher the exact story line then don't worry about it, once you know the general story arch just sit back and enjoy. I have a feeling the artist's intention was to be a bit ambiguous anyway.<br /><br />This effort comes across more as a cinematic soundtrack or even a Broadway musical score than a pop album, which is a good thing. If you like variety when it comes to mood/subject matter then \\"The ArchAndroid\\" is definitely for you. I heard everything from folk to psychedelic rock which, for listeners unfamiliar with artistic/musical outliers, might disturb the casual listener.<br /><br />Personally, I found very little to dislike here with a few of my favorites being \\"Sir Greendown\\", a song about our cyborg heroine falling in love with a human aka Anthony Greendown; very whimsical and adds a 'fairytale' element to Monae's futuristic chronicle. \\"57821\\" is a folksy ballad reminiscent of Simon and Garfunkle's 'Scarborough Fair'. \\"Oh, Maker\\" is Cindy's letter to God reflecting on the wonders of love and the pain of heartbreak: \\"Oh maker have you ever loved/or known just what it was/I can't imagine the bitter end/of all the beauty that we're living in\\". \\"Tightrope\\" is the Outkast influenced radio friendly track featuring the Big Boi himself, Antwan Patton; a funky uptempo grove with one of the best beats I've heard in a while. \\"BaBopByeYa\\" is the album's finale and what a finale; Monae's vocals soar over a somber jazz arrangement in three movements pining for...well...I don't want to give away the ending, of course. Additionally, the instrumentals are thankful respites from the heart-wrenching (though not unpleasant) drama and seem to create balance in keeping with traditional orchestral compositions. Poetic lyrics coupled with intelligent arrangements (eg: heavy breathing laced throughout \\"Come Alive\\" really makes you feel as though she's 'coming alive') create a harmonious balance between contemporary and archaic styles.<br /><br />I honestly can not stop listening to this album it is such a breath of fresh air! I have been waiting for something like this to come along for a while now and Janelle Monae has outdone herself and should be very proud. The breadth and depth of creativity inherent within \\"The ArchAndroid\\" is virtually untouchable, especially when considering the air-headed fare produced by Morae's contemporaries. And while the Grammys snub was a bit dissapointing, her performance of \\"Cold War\\" encapsulated the  spirit and energy epitomized by this masterful work of art. I encourage you to check out any of her live performances on YouTube and/or pick up this gem. I guarantee you will be in for a treat!d in a while. \\"BaBopByeYa\\" is the album's finale and what a finale; Monae's vocals soar over a somber jazz arrangement in three movements pining for...well...I don't want to give away the ending, of course. That the book deals with the final weekly meetings between author Mitch Albom and his dying mentor/professor Morrie Schwartz (Brandeis U) has already been covered in detail in other reviews, so I'll just comment on the perceived value of chronicling these meetings in a book. I bought and read this book because it promised to relate to the readers the lifelong wisdom of a dying man, but I was sorely disappointed. His central revelation is that love is the most important thing in life. We must forgive. We must not let the capitalist system mislead us. We must be fair to all. We are all equal. People are inherently good but society and capitalism makes them bad. We are all one family. Impending death teaches us how to live. Be compassionate. Respect should be part of love. Even if I were to consider only the process of the author meeting with his dying professor every week for very personal conversations, I would still say I did not find much to enlighten me since these meetings revealed simply how Morrie makes the best of it by seeing a multitude of friends and family and optimistically encouraging them to live life to the fullest (of course, within the liberal/leftist view of life). So, if you are a liberal/leftist you probably will find this book a validation of principles dear to your heart. But, if you believe as I do that there are many ways of dealing with life and death besides this one, then you will come to realize that perhaps our political and moral ideologynot only can guide us in life but can dictate how we see our impending death as well. In today's world of polarized ideas, politics, religions, morals, and ideologies, it is hard to envision that there is a universal lesson to be learned from any dying man, in spite of what this book promises to deliver. As wonderful and loving as Professor Morrie is deemed to have been, his deathbed wisdom is inseparable from his political ideology, a fact we need to consider when we search for universal truths in those with exemplary lives.
