B004VUP8WK|5.0|In the taxonomy of extreme metal, there is nary a shadowy crawlspace for subjectivity. No one has a seizure when they look at best-of lists and see \\"Altars of Madness\\".  The rough parameters of the style were established crusty eons ago by people that played the music that they would want to listen to, and by accident, what fans wanted to hear.  The great bands, old and new, rise like flame to the heavens and are recognized.  If this comes off as a bit fascistic, I agree that your soul is free to live in whatever illusion it desires.  But there is no freedom without truth-- and the truth is that this album is jaw-dropping, use once and destroy awesome.<br /><br />*Too Extreme is an ecstatic shock to the senses.  It brings to mind hard-hitting electro-house and London bass as much as hardcore techno. By the ears of an old-school raver, new school desert burner, this is manifestly state-of-the-art. (For a link between Burning Man and heavy metal, consider the lyrics of \\"Flame to the Surface\\" by Cryptopsy).<br /><br />*I Am Morbid is the premier rallying cry for death metal unity.  In a parallel dimension anyway, where the listeners are highly sophisticated.  It could also be a reply to the title of Tom G. Warrior's book about Celtic Frost.<br /><br />*Destructos Vs. The Earth is the only true answer to Iron Man ever made.  If you want something to stick in your craw, listen to the version of Iron Man on Nativity in Black II.<br /><br />*Nevermore has sounds (guitar?!) that reminded me of golden age techstep drum & bass.  Azagthoth is the Tom Morello of death metal.<br /><br />It has come to my attention that some have failed to find enjoyment in this album.  If this album did not sit well with you, perhaps it will be some consolation to know that there are serious metalheads who worship material like this, which only a band of Morbid's caliber can deliver.  It might suggest a departure from the usual chemical formulas (those spelled out in \\"Goddamn Electric\\" by Pantera).  But the exquisite panache that this album radiates is the result of musical genius married to decades of performance.<br /><br />Let's consider the matter of lyrics.  Traditional death metal lyrics are a reductionist study of the human body and its frailties, or alternatively, metaphysical speculation as to the limitless power of the mind.  Vincent takes the latter path most often, channeling the voice of a vainglorious, deranged deity calling his faithful to celebrate.  His magniloquence is unmatched in the scene; he is a hero, a leader, he chants in Latin for gods' sake.  Here, the brilliant camp and irony in Vincent's lyrics is executed with Tarantino -levels of style and confidence.  It brings to mind the Mars of Edgar Rice Burroughs or the Conan of Robert E. Howard.  While we are on the subject, have you ever read the lyrics to Beneath the Remains-- or The Negation, cherished treasures that they are?  The, ahem, outsider's take on our language is part of the charm, not to mention an alien challenge to our conceptual scheme. (Incidentally, the newest Decapitated has some of the best lyrics ever to grace a metal album, penned by Jarek Szubrycht).<br /><br />I'm going to be honest with you-- outside of interludes, 95% of the time I have a serious problem with electronics-- even keyboards-- being used in extreme metal.  Aside from a few tracks on Remanufacture, it just has never sounded right.  That is all changing now.  I'm not sure if it is a result of advances in studio technology or the heightened sophistication of the artists, but for empirical proof of the evolution, you need only hear the terrifying sub-bass of Bring Me The Horizon's dubstep remixes, the shocking studio manipulations on the new Whitechapel, or the minimalist, industrial keyboards of Daath.  Or this album-- though be aware that Tim Yeung provides most of the drum hits.<br /><br />Finally, I would be remiss if I did not point out that your ears will not be murdered by faux-opera singing on this.<br /><br />After I had drafted up this review,  I saw that Metal Hammer had reviewed this and gave it 9/10 stars.  I was relieved, but at the same time felt a little less special.  Guard against that feeling.  If everyone in the world loved metal, would you hate it?  That would be a sad comment on existence, but such abstract questions need not trouble us.  This is a marginalized art form that should be hailed with as much fanfare as classical music was in ages past, with its patrons kings and queens.t Metal Hammer had reviewed this and gave it 9/10 stars. B0012GN47C|4.0|As I have indicated in other reviews, Santana has demonstrated a propensity to release music as album styles, in groups of three.  For instance the first three Santana albums were of the classic \\"Santana Rock\\" period, the three formed a triumvirate of new powerful rock music that dominated the charts.  The music on all three albums progressed easily from a fantastic debut to an incredible pinnacle of performance, but the core style of music on the three held them together as a package.  The next three Santana albums were the \\"new\\" Santana, spiritual and fusion jazz driven.  They did not have the commercial success of the first three, but in the jazz circles and from the pens of critics, the applause was mighty!  AMIGOS now begins another trio of albums (studio releases 7, 8, and 9 respectively) that some consider the \\"festival\\", or \\"carnival\\" (in the Latin sense of the word) trio.  These are happy albums, filled with fun and dance music.  Santana's interpretive improvisational styles are still key in these albums, and they draw heavily from both the core classic rock period of Santana, and from the most recent funk-fusion-jazz period.  AMIGOS marks another corner for Carlos and band to turn, a new street to play on.  AMIGOS charted in the top 10 on the charts (barely) which attests to the return to more commercial venues, so perhaps Bill Graham had something to do with pushing Carlos back from the jazz circles, but admittedly, AMIGOS and the follow-up FESTIVAL are a mixed bag (however a good mixed bag for solid Santana fans).<br /><br />AMIGOS attempted two singles, a truncated version of Dance Sister Dance which failed to crack the charts, and Let It Shine which barely cracked the charts, peaking at #77 for one week.  One would suspect that Santana had failed in the attempt to return to commercial success, but AMIGOS did grab album chart position #10 for a couple weeks, and there is no denying that the two best compositions on the album are stalwart standards of the Santana fan base as well as concert staples.  Dance Sister Dance is, despite the lack of chart success (the single version was seriously flawed), is a vision of both dance hall Santana feel-good music and during and after the transmutation (which marks the typical Santana compositions) an incredible guitar-keyboard-percussion trip.  Europa, upon first listen in 1976, was and is easily identifiable as a song which would come to mark the definition of just what Santana really is.  It is probably one of the most played concert songs, it has been in constant rotation on FM stations and has appeared on numerous \\"best of\\", live, and instrumental compilations, and is arguably one of, if not the, most popular Santana single (despite the fact that it was not released as a chart single).  Europa is a solemn and emotional guitar solo with ethereal keyboards, somewhat an evolutionary extension of Samba Pa Ti, it was first composed when Carlos witnessed a friend having a bad trip on mescaline.  Keyboardist Tom Coster assisted Carlos with some of the chord progressions and the song was born.  These two songs alone are worth the purchase of the CD at the budget price, especially considering the remastered sound quality, easily accomplished since the original masters for release of the album were of the highest caliber by 1976 standards.  Take Me With You, Let Me, and Guitano, are all more than mere acceptable entries to the Santana canon, they each bring (brought) a rather new slant on the Santana sound, including Spanish guitar, funky R&B and gospel oriented Latin rock.  The real losers on the album are two good attempts which just fall flat with most listeners:  the failed single Let It Shine (a Sly & Family Stone funk and gospel attempt with good intentions that you might tolerate or even like depending on your own ears), and the terrible preachy Tell Me Are You Tired of which I really have nothing good to say.  Both songs begin with promise but quickly fall into disarray.<br/><br />If you do not have Europa and/or if you do not have the 8 minute version of Dance Sister Dance, I would highly recommend this album with the confidence that you will enjoy at least half if not more of the album (who knows, you might love it all).  AMIGOS is a definitive sounding album in the Santana catalog, it is by no means, a poor album (for that, avoid Beyond Appearances and Freedom in '85 and '87 respectively).  There is however, an easy way to bypass both AMIGOS and the follow-up FESTIVAL and still keep the best elements of both, and that is to purchase the glorious double disc album release, MOONFLOWER which is easily one of the best Santana albums ever.  Check out my review there!  If you are a completist like me, and have been exchanging your vinyl for CD over the years or if you never bought this album and were wondering if the other tunes sitting with the Big 2 on this package were any good, I would say go for it.do not have Europa and/or if you do not have the 8 minute version of Dance Sister Dance, I would highly recommend this album with the confidence that you will enjoy at least half if not more of the album (who knows, you might love it all). B0058ZPNOK|5.0|Take a linear narrative, throw in a dash of chaos, and you end up with Hesher, a film that debuted at the Sundance Film Festival in 2010 but didn't see an official theatrical release until 2011. Playing with the person-helps-grieving-family storyline we've seen countless times, this is one of those indie dramas that assembles a cast of well-known actors and allows them to branch out from what's expected of them. The story, whether good or bad, functions as an opportunity for actors to play against type in a way that a typical mainstream movie wouldn't allow. Hardly a surprise, Hesher opened to mixed reviews with the majority of praise going to the actors and the majority of complaints going to the script and film itself. Well, with performances of this caliber and a story that is stronger than it has been given credit for; this film is actually quite successful.    T.J. (Devin Brochu) and his father Paul (Rainn Wilson) have recently suffered a traumatic loss that leaves them living with T.J.'s grandmother (Piper Laurie) and retreating into depression. T.J., who has found himself frequently tormented at school, becomes reclusive from those around him, while Paul wanders around the house in a prescription drug-induced daze. One day, T.J. stumbles upon Hesher (Joseph Gordon-Levitt), whose name and presence seem to be accompanied by the music of Metallica. In a classic example of deus ex machina, Hesher appears out of nowhere, with no back-story, and no reason to be in the film. He looks like a Grunge-era reject, drives a beat-up old van, has an upraised middle finger tattooed on his back, and seems to embrace chaos. He's also indifferent to societal rules; willfully disrespectful, vulgar, and violent. Despite T.J.'s protests, he moves into T.J.'s house and no one seems to care. During this time, T.J. meets Nicole (Natalie Portman), a young woman who captures his attention but turns her own attention to Hesher.     There are some inconsistencies in the script, particularly in regards to the title character. There is no logical reason for him to be in this film and his entire existence is disconnected from everything around him. The writers struggle to stay true to this strange character as he seems abnormally out-of-this-world one moment and like a human being with feelings and a conscience the next. It's a predictable move for this film to shape him from an ominous, violent thug into a caring, gentle soul; but luckily Gordon-Levitt is a gifted enough actor to make this transformation believable. Levitt crossed the threshold from child actor to real actor long ago, so there's no need to provide further proof about his acting ability. Regardless, Hesher is a great character role despite the inability of the film to define just who/what the character is. Once Levitt appears in character, it's impossible to take your eyes off him and who else but Levitt could make Hesher's speech at the end reach such a level of comic poignancy? Aside from Hesher, who is only truly developed from the performance rather than the script, the characters here are very well-developed and are brought to life marvelously.     This is the second time this year that Wilson has branched out from his Office character to prove what a gifted actor he is. There's very little humor in his character, giving Wilson the chance to stretch his acting muscles and bring Paul through a seamlessly convincing character arc. Brochu is a gifted young talent who brings some real, convincing emotional depth to the character of T.J. and Laurie, as the grandmother, is a lovely addition to the cast.    Hesher is an unapologetically weird little film that is destined to be misunderstood by most audiences. This little oddity of an indie drama is dark and violent, but ultimately sweet and hopeful and even funny at times.  By the time Hesher reaches the end of his character arc, it's all a bit contrived, but, call me crazy, I was quite fond of it. It plays with a traditional formula, isn't afraid to take chances, and boasts some very memorable performances. It's no masterpiece, but it's an enticing, satisfying film that kept me glued to the screen for its entire running time.    GRADE: A-

B000P0J09C|4.0|Full Metal Jacket is a unique entry in the Stanley Kubrick canon, as it's not quite a masterpiece but is as iconic and frequently discussed as his other films. Released in 1987, seven years after his previous film The Shining, Kubrick tackles the then-popular subject of the Vietnam War but puts his own Kubrickian spin on it. At 117 minutes, Full Metal Jacket was the shortest film Kubrick had made since 1964 despite a first and second half that seem like two different films.    As if any introduction is necessary, the film opens with Marine Corps recruits entering training at Parris Island and meeting Gunnery Sergeant Hartman (R. Lee Ermey), who intends to turn the recruits into hardened marines during their stay. Even those who haven't seen the film know about the opening scene, featuring Gunnery Sergeant Hartman meeting the recruits and spouting some oft-quoted dialogue that has become a part of the cultural vernacular. The first seven minutes is arguably one of the greatest opening scenes in film history. It's certainly one of the most iconic. Out of all the recruits, the film centers attention on Private Joker (Matthew Modine) and Private Leonard Lawrence (Vincent D'onofio), a simple, overweight recruit who incurs Hartman's wrath and is nicknamed \\"Gomer Pyle.\\" As Hartman begins putting the recruits through their training, he endlessly harasses the simple-minded Private Pyle, eventually pairing him up with Joker in the hopes that it will improve Pyle's skill. These scenes at Parris Island feature some of the most hilarious, depressing, and fascinating stuff that Kubrick ever shot and it's all over after a mere 45 minutes.     I'm going to go out on a limb here and say that the first 45 minutes of Full Metal Jacket is an undisputed masterpiece. After the Parris Island scenes, it essentially becomes a different film with the locale switching abruptly from South Carolina to Vietnam circa January of 1968. Here, Joker is a journalist accompanied by Private First ClassRafterman (Kevyn Major Howard), a combat photographer. With the beginning of the Tet Offensive, Joker and Rafterman are sent to cover the enemy attacks in South Vietnam. Here, Joker is reunited with Cowboy (Arliss Howard), one of the recruits at Parris Island, now a Sergeant and second-in-command of the Lusthog Squad. We're introduced to several members of the Lusthog Squad, most memorably Animal Mother (Adam Baldwin) a machine gunner, who's given some of the most interesting dialogue about the war effort.  The two halves of Full Metal Jacket are so disassociated from each other it's hard to believe, at times, they're from the same film. The second half is strong, very strong in fact, and effectively entertaining; but it fails to live up to the momentum of the first half and is simply not as memorable as what came before it. Even so, the climactic standoff with the sniper is one of the strongest scenes in the entire film.    England was used as a substitute for Vietnam and is beautiful at times, while at others Kubrick's Vietnam more closely resembles a post-apocalyptic wasteland. Even with the onslaught of Vietnam-related films that had been coming out of Hollywood and picking up accolades for the several years leading up to this film's release, not a single frame of this film seems overly familiar. Kubrick's composition of the most minor, seemingly insignificant shot was and remains awe-inspiring. Kubrick's Vietnam film may not be the best Vietnam film, but it certainly has some of the most memorable imagery. Oddly enough, there is something lacking from this film in regards to Kubrick's style. Perhaps it's the lack of classical music that characterizes so many of Kubrick's films and really lend to the atmosphere and style, but Full Metal Jacket, as a whole, lacks the distinct Kubrickian atmosphere that can be spotted in all of his output from 1968 on even by the most untrained eye. There are the unbroken, static shots that seem so still even when there's movementas well as the dead-stare of characters descending into madness that give him away, but there's a tone that's noticeably missing from this film.    Based on the novel The Short-Timers by Gustav Hasford, who co-wrote the script with Kubrick and Michael Herr, the film is not overtly political and lacks a defined pro or anti stance on war, but that doesn't make it a straight-forward war film either. With Joker sporting the iconic words \\"Born to Kill\\" on his helmet, while wearing a peace sign on his flak jacket and discussing the duality of man (\\"the Jungian thing\\") with his Major, there's enough of the high-minded here to give a Kubrick disciple plenty to analyze. However, it's no wonder that out of Kubrick's other efforts Full Metal Jacket has arguably been most embraced by the average moviegoer and teenager.     Full Metal Jacket features some stunning and unforgettable performances, particularly R. Lee Ermey (credited without the \\"R.\\") and D'onofio. There is absolutely nothing I can say about Ermey's brilliant performance that hasn't already been said and D'onofrio too has received his share of acclaim for his sympathetic, tragic performance. There's plenty of great acting across the board here, but the acting is less consistent than one would expect from a Kubrick film (him being such a perfectionist). There are very minor, almost unnoticeable moments where an actor will briefly appear, unconvincingly deliver his dialogue, and disappear. There is nothing glaringly bad, but there are a few moments where the actors just don't seem up to snuff.    This is overall a lesser film for Stanley Kubrick, the only one of his films released between 1969 and 1999 that isn't a masterpiece. With that in mind, this shows what a phenomenal filmmaker Kubrick was as most directors would hope that one of their lesser films was of this quality. It's not that Full Metal Jacket isn't a masterpiece in comparison to his other films, it's just lacking in certain areas that keep it from being a masterpiece itself. There's a lack of cohesion between the two parts of the film; they exist so far apart from each other that it almost feels like a great film was cut short and a good film, with some great scenes, was edited onto it. It's a near-masterpiece and an undeniable cultural landmark, a film that will stand the test of time among Kubrick's other work. The biggest problem with Full Metal Jacket is that is has scenes many will remember forever and others they'll quickly forget.    GRADE: B

1400032717|5.0|Review of The Curious Incident of the Dog in the Night-Time            As a student in the modern world, I have met and befriended many autistic children within various school systems. Never, though, had I been able to understand just how their minds were any different from mine. Thankfully, Mark Haddon came up with an answer by writing his award-winning The Curious Incident of the Dog in the Night-Time, a heart-wrenching and brilliant murder mystery that follows a fifteen-year-old autistic boy named Christopher John Francis Boone on what turns out to be the roller coaster ride of a lifetime.     Christopher's adventure begins in his neighborhood in Swindon, Wiltshire, when he finds his neighbor's dog, named Wellington, speared by a pitchfork in its owner's front yard. After being incarcerated (only partly) under the suspicion that he is the murderer, Chris decides to investigate the crime. Facing pressure from his father, a widower named Ed Boone, to stop investigating, it soon becomes clear that no savant could have predicted the sheer size and impact that Christopher's curious case has on his own life.     There is more to enjoy from this book than the plot, however. Through his own experience with special-needs children, Mark Haddon composes the perfect voice for Christopher, establishing his life and mindset to be ever-reasoning, able to find an interesting explanation for everything. Even some of the less profound parts of the book makes one realize just how confusing our behaviors can be to a completely rational person, for example, his description of a sigh: \\". . . It can mean that you are relaxed, or that you are bored, or that you are angry, and it all depends on how much air comes out of your nose and how fast and what shape your mouth is . . . and how you are sitting and what you said just before and hundreds of other things which are too complicated to work out in a few seconds\\" (15).     With these interesting explanations, though, also comes a sub-plot: Christopher is a math whiz. In fact, he is the first person to take the math A-level in his entire school. Not only that, but he manages to find a mathematical way to explain everything, be it rabbit populations, faults of intuition, battle tactics, or how to make the choices in your life. Overall, these deviations, while helping to support statements within the text, are also quite entertaining and insightful, and prove to be great, enlightening additions to the story.     Furthermore, with Christopher's tale, Haddon paints a picture to portray the thin line between truth and lies that exists in our society. As each part of the mystery unfolds, another fallacy is revealed about Christopher's life, another hardship uncovered in raising a special-needs child, and while it is heartbreaking to see the ultimate effects of the truth, Mark Haddon also makes an intriguing statement with his writing: the truth doesn't always have a happy or simple connotation. An easy, yet fascinating book to read, The Curious Case of the Dog in the Night-Time is highly recommendable, enjoyably stimulating, and reveals quite a lot of important information on the secret lives of today's misunderstood autistic children.

I don't understand the apparent desire for a sudden change of  persona from a portion of her fan base, even with the changes in her personal life. Especially given the immense success of past club single \\"Single Ladies\\". Beyonce is certainly in her comfort zone in this album.<br /><br />The album has an incredibly diverse sound but manages to blend well and maintain an identity. The 80's influenced \\"Party\\" relies heavily on a thick vocal harmony and a catchy saw synth pad/bass combo with simplistic synth drums providing the rhythm in the background. Beyonce keeps it in the 80's realm with the upbeat groovy jam \\"Love on Top\\". Sexy horns accent her vocals for much of the song while Beyonce croons about her love being the priority in her lovers life. \\"Rather Die Young\\" continues the trend of throwback old era influence with production and vocal arrangements stripped straight from the 70's.<br /><br />Beyonce takes it to the club with the catchy \\"Countdown\\", \\"End Of Time\\",  and \\"Run The world (girls)\\". All with a minimalist production with militaristic, bombastic, marching band appeal. Of these I'm partial to \\"End Of Time\\" with it's melodic sing songy chorus and blaring horn arrangement. \\"Run This World\\" while bound to be a hit in clubs wasn't the best choice for single as it's not as immediately appealing as her past singles.<br /><br />The album slows down with the vocally ambitious \\"1+1\\", \\"Best Thing I Never Had\\" and \\"I Was Here\\". \\"1+1\\" doesn't quite live up to the ambitions set forth as the vocal inflections begin to sound slightly annoying, especially during live performances but it provides a decent but somewhat forgettable ballad. \\"Best Thing I Never Had\\" is the most commercial of the bunch(and hence her 2nd single). A vengeful but very likable celebratory ode to a love that never was. While \\"I Was Here\\" has Beyonce proclaiming her desire to be remembered and having a great legacy. A mournful, plodding delivery leaves something to be desired but certainly a meaningful and heartfelt ballad.<br /><br />Other favorite is hard to pin down but very catchy \\"Start Over\\" with it's powerful and moving chorus. On the other hand \\"I Care\\" and \\"I Miss You\\" bring up the rear. While not bad they just prove to be filler material or to put it more politely, the most likely to be forgotten.<br /><br />As a whole I was very satisfied and impressed with this album. Vocally Beyonce brings it while only faltering on rare occasion. The production is great with throwback soul sounds, bombastic club anthems and melodic ballads. If you're expecting a transformed Beyonce that departs from her howling, bootylicious, hair swinging ways then you might look elsewhere for your R&B fix. But if you know the larger than life artist and performer Beyonce is and enjoy hearing her doing what she does best than I would completely recommend this album.but somewhat forgettable ballad. \\"Best Thing I Never Had\\" is the most commercial of the bunch(and hence her 2nd single). 0807014273|5.0|The book is one of the most important and significant works of the past millennium. The book is a story of Victor Franklin survival in the concentration camps and the lessons he picked up during his stay. There are two parts to the book, part 1 consists of his experiences, part 2 consists of his description of Logotherapy which he strongly advocated as a cure for many of the mental issues which people suffer from. The book presses the notion that a surge in various mental illness is due to the lack of any objective for our survival. We live empty lives, which are primarily in materialistic pursuits. The author cites Friedrich Nietzsche, \\"He who has a why to live for can bear almost any how\\", to promote the idea. While all of this may sound very superficial, philosophical, in all honesty, if you really have a mission to live for, and it matters a lot to you, the question of existence rarely arises. We make our mission as the meaning. This was in a way the tactics, Dr.Franklin used for survival in the camps. He made his mission helping the other patients, spreading the lessons he had learnt and so on.  He was very strongly driven by his objectives, and its no surprise that he survived the camps and went on to live till 1997.    I must confess, I enjoyed the first part of the book more than the second part, as it was primarily based on his experiences and observations. The 2nd part is a little tricky to follow if you are reading the book as a reader. You many find it more intuitive if you are professional trained for mental health and counseling.     Nevertheless, if you want to read a book which tries to give you correct hints at the path you should be taking in life, this is the perfect book. Its tries to offer a meaningful objective to your life, without pushing you towards any religion/faith.

It is Daenerys' destiny to reclaim the Iron Throne for the Targaryens, but doing so now means leaving her conquered city to her enemies and the slaves she has freed to their former masters. Meanwhile, her dragons are growing bigger and more unmanageable. As all the great houses make their bid for who will rule the Seven Kingdoms, north of the Wall the restless dead are gathering en masse to invade with the winter storms, adding slain victims to their ranks.<br /><br />Just as all the previous books in A SONG OF ICE AND FIRE have been, A Dance with Dragons is a tombstone-sized doorstopper of a book. (I purchased the Amazon Kindle version, and I swear my Kindle was heavier after I uploaded it.) An age has passed since A Game of Thrones kicked off what just might become the most influential series in fantasy since LORD OF THE RINGS. I've been anticipating A Dance with Dragons ever since I read the last sentence of the last book, A Feast for Crows, almost six years ago.<br /><br />A SONG OF ICE AND FIRE is a colossal-sized epic. There is no getting around the fact that this series is really one long book. I think Mr. Martin's idea of a conclusion for each book has been to build up to the most shockingly climactic outcome imaginable and then leave the readers in a nearly painful lurch of a cliffhanger. (Fair warning: the ending of A Dance with Dragons is no exception.) This is made almost intolerable by the fact that fans will be lucky to get more than one installment per decade.<br /><br />But if you can put all that aside and just takethe events as they happen in A Dance with Dragons, there's no denying it's another grand adventure.<br /><br />George R.R. Martin is one of those authors who could write the Yellow Pages and make it exciting. It's amazing how he can tell a story from so many unique perspectives. I'm not sure I even know as many people living in the real world as there are living and dying within these books.<br /><br />He draws the plot out like a sharp blade from a scabbard -- one that will easily cut the careless. His underlying theme that every hero is the other side's villain is profound and believable. All the players in this deadly game of thrones have a strategy that seems like a guaranteed victory. But since Martin solidly established early on, that no one -- I repeat, no one -- is safe, it's anyone's guess who will hold the Iron Throne when this is done. Providing there will even be a throne to win. That will depend on the Crows holding the Wall against the wights and other monsters that have yet to strike in full force.<br /><br />It almost seems impossible that Martin will actually be able to wrap this thing up with a worthy conclusion, and do it in both his and his readership's lifetime. Call me crazy, but I think he can. Afterwards, blurbs on fantasy book-covers will claim comparisons to George R.R. Martin and A SONG OF ICE AND FIRE, like they do now for J.R.R. Tolkien and LORD OF THE RINGS.

0553801473|4.0|Contrary to many, I am overall pretty satisfied with George RR Martin's A Dance With Dragons. Does it have problems? Well, yeah, and some of those are huge. But is it the worst book of the series, deserving of the derision and vitriol that's been heaped upon it by lots of reviewers? Certainly not. Allow me to elaborate.<br /><br />I won't recount the plot here (if plot is even the right word...meandering at times, achingly slow and then devastatingly wicked at others); there's entirely too much going on. Most of the chapters are Tyrion's, Jon's, and Dany's points of view, but others surface as well, including Theon, Davos, Jaime, Cersei, and Arya. There were a couple of POVs I must admit to being puzzled by, but I have to hope that ultimately Martin will bring them to a satisfying intricacy with the main characters. Would I have loved to know what Brienne and Sansa were up to? Sure, but it's all right. We're moving along, however slowly, and lots of Tyrion is always worth it.<br /><br />Biggest complaints? Well, the pacing is off in the first two hundred pages or so, with way too much detail given to traveling woes and feasting items. In any other series, these would be deal breakers. Do I think Martin's editor was snoozing? Probably. Just because you are uber successful doesn't mean you should be allowed to enumerate every food on a table. My other big, huge complaint is that it had been so long since I'd read A Feast For Crows, I was LOST for a hundred pages. That's my own fault for not rereading, but I also lay some blame on Martin, since five years between books is inexcusable.<br /><br />What I liked? Lots of things.The dialogue is perfect and the intrigue is thick. We got vintage Martin with double crosses, lots of gore, and set ups galore. Loved, loved, loved Theon's comeuppance and what he does later in the novel; loved Barristan Selmy's honor; loved the twist with Rhaegar's son. I found what happened with Dany and her dragons distasteful, yet entirely plausible; I know that the bloodletting has only barely begun after Cersei's humiliation. And poor Jon Snow...always trying to do the right thing, but it's usually at the wrong time and with the wrong people.<br /><br />Martin's world is convoluted, overblown, and nasty, much like the world we live in today. His writing, however, while needing editing, still draws one in and builds suspense, causing me to continue turning pages and luxuriating within the boundaries of the Seven Kingdoms and Beyond. Personally, I'm invested for the long haul, even if A Dance With Dragons pales when compared to A Storm of Swords. It's not the comparison I try to keep in mind, it's the journey.ible; I know that the bloodletting has only barely begun after Cersei's humiliation. The album flows from start to finish like any fine album should. Each of the 11 tracks are rich in lyrical detail and musically, the band use a mixture of rhythms and atmospheres with the guitars drenched in 60's style reverb and jangling as they should.<br /><br />Right from the opener \\"She's Thunderstorms\\" the band connect in a huge way as the song opens with mysterious open guitar notes followed by the chords that just make perfect sense. B004WKOCP8|4.0|Almost every band has had at least one album that polarizes the fan base during their career. The one album (or, if it's an artist with a very long career it might be a few albums) that gets debated about for years with those who will tear it to shreds claiming that is a pile of garbage and should never be listened to, and those who will defend it to the end, claiming that an artist needs to grow and try new things and not remain predictable or stagnant during a lengthy career.<br /><br />We have such a case with the latest Incubus effort \\"If Not Now, When?\\", an album that has certainly got the fanbase talking. \\"If Not Now, When?\\" is the band's first studio album in 5 years and there are those who say it should've been another 5 years before it was released, if ever. It's always a tough call for an artist once they've established a sound and a career. An artist never wants to be pigeonholed, but to veer too far away from what they are best known far can be career suicide. There are those who have done so with great success, but there are far more examples of failure with such a maneuver.<br /><br />Have Incubus committed career suicide with \\"If Not Now, When?\\" Have they decided to become a sad incarnate of the dreadful Maroon 5 or Matchbox 20? I say no. Although at least half of the fans are crying treason, this is a very thoughtful, melodic, beautifully constructed piece of art. \\"If Not Now, When?\\" kicks things off in sterling fashion. Sure, it doesn't remotely rock and inches very closely to Adult Contemporary, but it is a stunningly ethereal song awash in sublime melodies and Brandon Boyd's vocals soar to the heavens. The slight guitar and keyboard textures add to the beauty as do the lyrics. If this was a mere sellout, it wouldn't have been so carefully constructed, nor would Boyd sing with such passion. When he states, \\"I have waited all my life, If not now, when will I? Stand up and face the bright light, Don't hide your eyes, it's time\\" how can the listener not be swept away?.<br /><br />The mellow sounds continue with tracks such as \\"Promises, Promises\\", \\"Isadore\\", \\"Thieves\\", \\"Friends And Lovers\\" and the closer \\"Tomorrow's Fool\\" which has some great vocal harmonies and a bit of a Pink Floyd feel. \\"The Original\\" is another largely mellow song but takes a surprisingly heavy, Progressive Rock turn around the 3 and a half minute mark. Most impressive is the nearly 8 minute long epic \\"In The Company Of Wolves\\" which is also fairly Progressive, slowly building to a long crescendo with fuzzy guitars, eerie atmospherics, distorted vocals, electric piano and fat rhythms after starting rather generic for the first 4 minutes or so.<br /><br />So, does anything sound remotely like vintage Incubus? Well, \\"Switchblade\\" gets kind of funky with impressive bass and drum work, and lead single \\"Adolescents\\" pretty much rocks and has solid guitar work. (No wonder this was chosen as lead single, as perhaps label panic made this an obvious choice to mislead the public). There's no DJ-ing, no Hip Hop and no Nu Metal. It's up to the listener to decide if Incubus have grown or lost their minds, but \\"If Not Now, When?\\" is one of the year's most pleasant surprises.?. B0045ZAQSM|5.0|This is a German production, with English subtitles. The star is Adam Bousdoukos, who masterfully portrays Zinos Kazantsakis, owner of the Soul Kitchen Restaurant in Hamburg. There seems to be a small, but loyal, customer base of working-class folk who like cold beer.  The building is old and poorly maintained. We get the impression that the Soul Kitchen is doing little more than scraping along.    Zinos is an honest man, a loyal man, straightforward in the way he handles people.  His instinct is to trust others. Too bad that some of the people close to him don't share these qualities. His brother is a problem (big problem) gambler. His beautiful girlfriend leaves him for a job assignment in China.  He hires a chef (a chef in this joint?) who has an explosive temper and turns out haute cuisine that drives away faithful customers.      I don't want to say more about the plot, but the big question becomes: Can Zinos handle all that life throws at him? Life throws all kinds of stuff at him. There's a lot more than what I hint at above.    This production is often tagged as a comedy. But I did not laugh much at all.     Watch it and see how funny you think it is.

0545259088|5.0|At one point in FOREVER, Maggie Stiefvater's wrap-up to her bestselling Wolves of Mercy Falls trilogy, Cole St. Clair --- the troubled but ultimately pragmatic bad boy of the group --- says, \\"Sometimes I was just never sure there was supposed to be a happy ending.\\" Indeed, at several points in FOREVER, the kind of ending Stiefvater will leave readers with is very much in doubt. Given the series's complexity, emotional authenticity, and numerous twists and turns, no one would expect it to end with butterflies and songbirds --- unless they'd had their tiny innards ripped out of their chests by bloodthirsty wolves, of course. But, as will surprise no one who's read the first two books in this superb series, Stiefvater manages to pull together the trilogy in a way that is suspenseful but also true to the story and its characters.    At the novel's opening, all the characters are in crisis. Grace has undergone her first traumatic, dangerously unpredictable transformation into a wolf, and is more traumatized by her short-lived returns to human form --- at which point she becomes painfully aware of all that she's lost and still losing. When she's a wolf, of course, she's fully canine. Lacking language and memory, she's filled with pure sensation and unable to grieve.    Her boyfriend, Sam, who underwent a dangerous experimental procedure to become --- and stay --- human, is grieving enough for the both of them. Every day is full of uncertainty and increasing danger, as Grace's transformations become more erratic and the threat to all the wolves grows. Spearheading the effort is Isabel's father, Mr. Culpeper, who's enlisted powerful politicians and businesspeople to back his plans to shoot wolves with high-powered rifles from low-flying helicopters. Isabel fears for the lives of her friends, especially Cole, who's currently in human form. But no one appears safe from this new human threat, not to mention the danger posed by the increasingly psychotic wolf Shelby has become.As for Cole, he's enjoying (as much as he enjoys anything) pursuing the elusive cure that could just possibly save them all.    Tension mounts as perils seem to arise on every side, with no obvious or easy answer in sight. Ultimately, in a move that may generate either salvation or heartbreak, the friends try to find Sam's father figure, Beck, the one who may hold their last best chance for lasting peace.    Maggie Stiefvater's previous books about Mercy Falls have been both achingly romantic and unfailingly authentic, and FOREVER is no exception. To the end, she remains true to her characters and to the mythology she's created, which, as she indicates in an author's note, includes rendering the wolves' lifelike behavior, for better or for worse. The course of true love might run even more roughshod for werewolves, and the future for Grace and Sam, Isabel and Cole is far from certain at the novel's close. But at least there's hope, and a satisfying conclusion for Stiefvater's many fans.        --- Reviewed by Norah Piehl

Born in captivity, Jack has never been outside (the) Room, a heavily fortified shed in the maniac's backyard. 1453767533|5.0|I'm not sure why this novel is considered the \\"lesser\\" of the Austen novels.  It's brilliantly funny and contains wonderful satire of the gothic romance novels popular at the time and the silly games that suitors play with one another.  Through the character of Isabella Thorpe, Austen reveals the damages that can come from a deceptively manipulative friendship.  It is only when our heroine Catherine Morland becomes independent from Isabella's influence that she can realize that she wasn't a true friend at all.<br /><br />I have great affection for Catherine Morland.  It is true that she's the least intelligent of Austen's heroine's but that's because this is a true coming of age story.  We get to see her journey from naive young girl, to an independent young woman, through her profound life experiences.  And Henry Tilney is a great hero because even when he is amused and befuddled by Catherine, he does everything he can to spare her feelings and lead her in a sensible direction.  Plus he's really funny.<br /><br />It has been years since I'd read this book and I forgot how many of Austen's best quotes come from it! Here are just a few:<br /><br />\\"But when a young lady is to be a heroine, the perverseness of forty surrounding families cannot prevent her. Something must and will happen to throw a hero in her way.\\"<br /><br />\\"Friendship is certainly the finest balm for the pangs of disappointed love.\\"<br /><br />\\"I cannot speak well enough to be unintelligible.\\"<br /><br />Catherine's thoughts on reading History:<br />\\"I read it a little as a duty, but it tells me nothing that does not either vex or weary me. B000FVQM2Y|5.0|What more can you say than that this is the best, most masterfully executed translation of the classic noir genre into any other period?     The script is breathtakingly smooth in its fully and unapologetically stylized manner. The characters play their characters gorgeously, and the few moments where the noir facade is broken to point out that these are, theoretically, teenagers (when the mob boss' mother serves the protagonist a glass of milk before they get down to talking the business of drugs, crime, and espionage) work wonderfully to complement the mood.    To make it perfectly clear: this is not a movie attempting to be in any way realistic. It is completely and utterly stylized. Perhaps best compared with Rian Johnson's other film, Brothers Bloom, which is introduced by a sequence describing the brothers' childhood and beginning as con men in a kind of tall tale/folk tale rhyme. Each film lives in a world which follows very different rules than our own, but a world which is constructed with incredible skill and delicacy.    Brick is entirely self-aware, and it weaves a deliciously dark tale of treachery, crime, passion, manipulation, and tragic downfall all set among teenagers in an average Californian high school. A classic I'm sure I'll re-watch countless times as I make sure all my best friends have seen it.

B000F39M9W|3.0|Hip and cool count for a lot in Hollywood culture, but there's not much glamour around classical music. Salonen took advantage of the opening of Walt Disney Hall to create excitement around L.A.'s formidable orchestra, and one tactic for bringing in the corwds was to boost Stravinsky's Rite of Spring into rock-concert status. It wasn't a desecration; the composer reveled in is own chic. For a while the LAPO actually had buzz in a city where classical concerts mostly draw older patrons from Pasadena. Salonen was already a cultural hero, and his dedication to modern music, as well as to embracing the larger community, offsets his lack of conviction in a wide range of standard repertoire, from Mozart, Haydn, and Beethoven through Brahms, Wagner, and Bruckner - I don't think he's even touched the first two, certainly not on recordings. The orchestra's connection with DG, which began with this album, has solidified under Gustavo Dudamel, Salonen's successor, whose credentials in reaching the community are incontestable, and so an honorable legacy continues.<br /><br />As for the CD itself, the Mussorgsky Night on Bald Mountain, played in its original version, is fast-paced and energized but not warm or Russian. It's more like a night on a scary dissection table. In almost every way the original is inferior to Rimsky-Korsakov's brilliant rewrite (don't expect a repeat of Boris Godunov, where the composer's first two versions speak in is voice without Rimsky's smooth touch), and it takes more than Salonen offers to convince me otherwise. More disappointing is his lightweight, clinical reading of Bartok's Miraculous Mandarin Suite. Solti gave the score electro-shock treatment, and perhaps Salonen thought it was time for a little tenderness, softening the shock value and turning down the lurid spotlights. It's not possible to make this assaualtive score mild-mannered; I wish Salonen hadn't tried.<br /><br />The main attraction, however, is the Rite, which was broadcast on PBS and touted as a miracle in the impressive acoustics of the new Hall. (Walt Disney does have the remarkable trait of enveloping the listener in sound, the effect being startlingly like listening to a high-end stereo system - you are almost at the movies.) On this disc I heard neither the \\"perfection\\" of the lead review or the fatal defects that someone else complains about. The Rite of Spring has been an engineer's treat and audiophile showpiece for well over fifty years. DG's new version is in that lineage, yet it's a serious reading from Salonen, skillfully shaped, with rhythmic precision and every detail in place.<br /><br />I wouldn't say that my blood heated up listening to it, however. Dg's new hotshot Dudamle has since recorded a more dramatic, visceral account. If you refer to great Stravinsky conductors like Bernstein and Boulez on Sony, with magnificent playing from the NY Phil. and the Cleveland Orch. respectively, Salonen seems a little underwhelming, although he finds some very quiet, spooky patches to highlight in Part Two, and the orchestral playing is lean and precise, a trademark of the Salonen era. Ultimately, however, this reading suffers forrom a lack of character, something to make it stand out form the pack.S and touted as a miracle in the impressive acoustics of the new Hall. B003QI95Y0|5.0|Clutch released their eighth studio outing From Beale Street To Oblivion in 2007, an album which saw the band include a lot more of the blues influences which were slowly creeping into the band's overall sound.<br /><br />For those who don't know, Clutch play rock music sounding somewhere between stoner and classic rock with a curious mixture of loose jam-band vibes and ultra tight precision performances. This album in particular sees the band adopt a stronger blues influence as well, for an even more diverse mixture of styles.<br /><br />Singer Neil Falon has an amazing voice which helps him come across as a crazed gospel singer one minute, a ragged blues man the next and then a hard rock front man at a moment's notice. The man's eclectic appeal is one of the band's primary selling points.<br /><br />The lyrics are truly spectacular; some really unique observations are made, complex historical and political references sit beside surrealist imagery and large doses of humor all mixed together on each album and often within each song. As well as the vocals and lyrics; the individual musicians are incredible, from the astounding rock solid drummer J.P Gastor to the subtle and interesting bassist and guitarist Dan Maines and Tim Sult, both of whom are ridiculously talented individuals.<br /><br />Beale Street' is a strong and consistent album; opening up with that fast and powerful double-punch of 'You Can't Stop Progress,' and 'Power Player,' the album rarely lets up, and is one of the band's most instant releases. The music on the album is a lot more simple and direct than on previous albums, although the record keeps the inclusion of keyboards by Mick Schauer as well, which were introduced on the band's previous album.<br /><br />Highlights include the lyrically brilliant 'The Devil And Me,' the bluesy 'Electric Worry' (which features Eric Oblander of Five Horse Johnson on harmonica) and the gigantic album closer 'Mr. Shiny Cadillackness,' all of which are fantastic songs, on an already strong album.<br /><br />Overall, From Beale Street To Oblivion to is a great release from an excellent band and I'd highly recommend that you check it out, although with caveat that the band have strayed very far away from their earlier sound. If you don't like the blues influences and would prefer something more similar to the band's oldest albums, maybe reconsider. For everyone else who just wants some amazing rock music, make sure to get yourself a copy. It begins when he finds his neighbor's dog dead in her yard. Along his journey he learns some things he did not know about before. While reading the book, the reader learns about the way people can sometimes behave irrationally to protect someone they care about, and the lengths they go to.    Although no one will ever fully know or understand how an autistic child thinks, this book gives some small insight about how difficult it can be to take care of someone with that condition. Christopher's day is based on the simple fact of how many cars of a certain color he sees in a row on the way to school.  He doesn't like lies, has a hard time registering people's emotions, and can't really process simple things as quick as people without autism. Because of Christopher's attention to detail, it is very easy to imagine the setting and the people around him.  Along with Christopher's view, readers also get to see a bit about how it's like for people living with or taking care of autistic children. Christopher's father tries his best to make life easier for him all while dealing with the loss of his wife. B004NDVJJG|5.0|Hersch, Fred.  Whirl. Palmetto Records. 2010<br />Fred Hersch, p; John H&eacute;bert, b; Eric Mcpherson, dr<br /><br />There seems to have emerged a broad synthesis or approach in modern jazz piano playing, not the avant garde edge playing of, say, Fred Van Hove or Marilyn Crispell, but mainstream modern, that is deeply rewarding to the listener. Pianists as diverse as Keith Jarrett and Paul Bley, Kenny Baron or Kenny Werner or Brad Mehldau improvise in this context with great fluency and inventiveness, and equally important, with great musicality. The elements of the synthesis are these: a foundation in bop harmonies with the incorporation of more modern harmonic elements (a touch of impressionism here, of modalism there), a pianistic approach to their instrument, and interplay with drums and bass that is reminiscent of but doesn't try to duplicate the shifting balance of harmonic and rhythmic responsibilities in the classic Bill Evans trio with Scott La Faro and Paul Motian.<br /><br />The difference is apparent upon comparison. Listen, for instance, to the two great last albums of John Lewis, Evolution and Evolution II (1999, 2001). Lewis was a master of subtly placed chords but his soloing, and much of his ensemble playing, is straight out bop style -single note and propulsive, though tempered in Lewis's case by his profound debt to Count Basie, as seen in the sparseness of Lewis's playing and his subtle placement of rhythmic accents across the pieces he plays.<br /><br />Two recent albums show the creative strength of the new post-bop but still basically bop piano synthesis.<br /><br />Fred Hersch is a talented and imaginative pianist who shines in almost every context. His recent album, Whirl, on which he is joined by talented drummer Eric McPherson and bassist John Hebert, shows his strengths to advantage. The program is intelligent and varied. There are two standards, \\"You're My Everything,\\" done between mid tempo and up, with killer drumming, and \\"When Your Lover Has Gone,\\" done slow and lush. There is a bolero written by Hersch, \\"Mandevilla.\\" The trio plays it like a traditional bolero -but still recognizably jazz--all the way through. I especially recommend a boppish single note piece entitled \\"Mrs. Parker of KC.\\" It was written by Hersch's teacher, the wonderfully talented and uncategorizable pianist-composer-teacher, Jaki Byard. This tune, and another entitled \\"Skipping,\\" are rhythmically the most interesting in the album. The title song, \\"Whirl,\\" Hersch dedicates to the great prima ballerina of the New York City Ballet, Suzanne Farrell. It is another of Hersch's trademark swirling melodies, with the drums boiling up and receding and then coming back in again like the waves in the ocean.<br /><br />All in all, this is a highly satisfactory album which validates Hersch's high reputation in modern jazz circles.<br /><br />Wasilewski, Marcin. Faithful. ECM. 2011<br />Marcin Wasilewski, p; Slawomir Kurkiewicz, b; Michal Miskiewicz, dr.<br /><br />This is my second album by the Wasilewski trio (he's issued three) and it is fully as good as the first, which is to say, superb. The trio members, who have played together since the very early 2000s when they backed their fellow Pole, trumpeter Tomasz Stanko, are almost empathic in the way they pick up on each other's music cues and play them back or counter them to accent the group sound. This is a very exciting group, as well as a musically sophisticated one.<br /><br />There is not one bad cut in this fine album but some stand out: the propulsive  \\"Night Train to You,\\" the trio's take on the seldom played standard, \\"The Ballad of the Sad Young Men,\\" the pensive \\"Oz Guiros\\" and \\"Woke Up in the Desert.\\" I like especially their take on Paul Bley's boppish \\"Big Foot.\\"<br /><br />Wasilewski and Hersch don't sound at all alike but they sound like they've come through the same school in some respects. There is the expanded use of the keyboard, and a greater and more varied use and placement of chords -in Hersch's case, whole solo stretches done chordally rather than in single note streams. In each group, the musicians listen to each other more intensely. The bass has been left off the leash of four beats to a bar thumping out chord bases, and the drums do more than keep the time. There seems in both cases, especially in Hersch's case, a greater willingness to draw on music other than jazz.<br /><br />Enough! Faithful is just an exceptional album. B002G1YPH0|5.0|This quite possibly could be the best air cooler for its price.  I recently purchased and installed a Hyper 212+ on a Core 2 Duo E8400 (LGA 775).  With the stock cooler, my average temperatures were:    Core 1 idle - 48C  Core 2 idle - 42C  Core 1 load - >60C  Core 2 load - 58C    After replacing the stock heatsink with the Hyper 212+, my average temps have dropped to:    Core 1 idle - 36C  Core 2 idle - 32C  Core 1 load - 48C  Core 2 load - 46C    This is excellent performance compared to the price.  Installation is typical for an aftermarket cooler - it will require you to remove your motherboard in order to mount the backplate.  However, it is not remarkably difficult.  Printed directions could be clearer, but are adequate to explain how to mount it.    Pros:  * Average temperature drop of 10-12C over stock cooling (in my case)  * Remarkably low cost for cooling performance  * Should fit in most mid or full towers (I would not recommend it for slimline cases)  * Expandable with second 120mm fan for push/pull setup - mounting brackets included  * Compatible with wide variety of CPU sockets, including LGA 775, 1155, 1156, and 1366, and AM2, AM2+ and AM3    Cons:  * Included directions may be unclear to someone inexperienced with installing aftermarket coolers  * While not \\"heavy,\\" heavy enough to require backplate, which means removing motherboard to mount    This is definitely a cooler I would recommend to anyone needing to improve their cooling solution without spending a huge amount.  It performs on par with many more expensive coolers, and its flexibility in socket support means you can keep it when you upgrade, at least for the immediate future.  There are better coolers out there for more money, but I don't think you can beat this one for the price.

0439023521|4.0|Sixteen-year-old Katniss lives in a bleak future Appalachia, renamed District 12 by the Capitol, a Rome-like city-state which keeps all the Districts on the edge of starvation to supply itself with fantastic wealth.  Katniss barely keeps her family alive by illegally hunting outside her town's decrepit fences with her childhood friend Gale.  Then she finds herself swept into the Hunger Games, the annual ceremony in which the Capitol forces the people of the twelve Districts to watch on live TV as two dozen of their children fight each other to the death in a huge wilderness arena, as a brutal proof that the Districts are completely at the Capitol's mercy.    Suzanne Collins's sense of narrative pace is faultless; the book flies along and can easily be finished in a night.  The plot is an interesting mix of inspirations, part Takami's Battle Royale, part Orwell's 1984, part vicious satire of reality TV.  Parts of it could well have been drawn directly from Collins's nightmare journal: the tongueless Avoxes, the tracker jacker wasps whose sting induces terrifying hallucinations, and worst of all, the grisly origin of the muttations bred by the Capitol.  Collins never lets us forget Katniss's constant pain, fear and resentment; this is not in any sense an adventure, but a murderous, traumatic ordeal.  Indeed, this science fiction book paints a better picture of what war feels like for the weaker side than any work of historical fiction that I know.  The people from whom Katniss most needs help are the very people that the Games' rules require her to kill, facing her with constant conflict throughout the book, and the one strain of hope that survives the story is that Katniss does manage to form bonds with some of the other fighters in the arena with her.    Collins may have been analogizing the wealthy Capitol and its impoverished Districts to America (or the rich world countries in general) and the Third World.  If so, the analogy is certainly not exact - America doesnot have anywhere near so strong a hold over poor countries as the Capitol does over the Districts - but in the Capitol's frivolity and indifference to the poverty that surrounds it, the parallel to the average American is uncomfortably close.    The book is certainly far from perfect.  The ending is predictable and the final obstacle proves much easier to surmount than it should have been; it is hard to believe that nobody in the previous 75 Games has ever done what Katniss does at the end here, or that the Capitol has not devised an answer to it.  Furthermore, Katniss's character development is weak; she is stillv ery recognizably the same person at the end of the Games that she was at the beginning.  Some may fault the book for lack of originality; as I have mentioned, its roots in Takami and Orwell are all too obvious.  But it is not a stale retread; Collins has taken what she has experienced and transformed it into something new.  Very much like a mockingjay.

B000002VJH|4.0|Blues for Allah is the Grateful Dead's experimental folk/jazz/psychedelic conglomeration.  In my opinion, it's far from a classic.  I don't feel this way just because it's a studio album.  I like studio Grateful Dead just fine and I honestly don't believe their live albums are *that* much better quality-wise.  I was honestly shocked going into Blues for Allah today.  I didn't read any reviews and instead just dove right in and listened to it, so I'm a bit surprised what it sounds like.      My main complaint is that the vocals are, for the most part, really weak.  In some instances the vocals CLEARLY don't belong in between the spaced out guitar trickery that runs through the album.      A good example is on the 12-minute \\"Blues for Allah\\" suite on side two.  The first 3 minutes are really mediocre because the vocal melody sounds so simplistic and corny.  I believe had this vocal melody been put into the possession of a British band, they would have made it cooler.  At least it improves once \\"Sand Castles & Glass Camels\\" comes in afterwards- this is because this particular aspect of the piece has some really hypnotic guitar work totally reminiscent of Jefferson Airplane's \\"Spare Chaynge\\" but not nearly as cool because it's already been done by several artists back in the 60's.  By 1975, the music scene had done changed... but don't tell these guys that.     Actually that's not entirely true- the fact such a guitar sound has been done before isn't the problem.  It really does sound like Jerry Garcia is trying desperately to sound like the psychedelic After Bathing At Baxter's period of the Jefferson Airplane on this particular track.  The entire thing winds down with more vocals during \\"Unusual Occurrences in the Desert\\" which are perhaps an improvement over the vocals we had to endure when the song began, but only a very slight improvement.  Anyway, despite my complaining, \\"Sand Castles & Glass Camels\\" is saved by featuring really cool guitar work.     \\"Help on theWay\\" actually resembles a disco song, or at the very least, the kind of glossy commercial pop you'd hear in abundance back in the mid 70's.   It still has a decent groove going for it, so it's passable.  \\"Slipknot\\" is a relatively forgettable jam of sorts.  It begins with these guitar parts too sparsely arranged to make much of an impression, but soon it develops.  The actual notes never stick with me, but it sounds nice.  \\"King Solomon's Marbles\\" is based around some brief, speedy guitar licks.  Nothing that blows me away but it's good enough.  \\"Stronger Than Dirt or Milk In the Turkey\\" gets a positive reception JUST for the hilarious song title alone!  Unless I'm overlooking something, this is basically the same thing as \\"King Solomon's Marbles\\" but with short dashes of jazzy keyboards and quick drum fills tossed in between the guitar licks.      Alright, time to mention the real meat and potatoes.  \\"Crazy Fingers\\" is 6 minutes long and not much of it is devoted to guitar soloing (if any).  It's just a REALLY catchy vocal melody with reggae beats moving the song forward.  It's a nice experiment for the band.  Speaking of experiments, \\"Sage & Spirit\\" is *drastically* different from anything the Grateful Dead has ever attempted before- a classical/flute instrumental.  A VERY good one, too.  Maybe my favorite song on the entire album.  \\"The Music Never Stopped\\" is some kind of country/dance affair with another strong vocal melody and, believe it or not, underrated guitar soloing (since it appears in all the right spots).  \\"Franklin's Tower\\" has the best chorus on the entire album, and if you don't know much about this band you might be surprised when you hear this great chorus and realize you've heard it somewhere before- it's a fairly popular song.       Overall, I like this album.  You're probably thinking \\"You don't REALLY like it or you'd, you know, write a nicer review!\\"  Well... that's true, haha.   Let me just say this- I admire the Grateful Dead's studio output.  I realize some fans will say I'm not experiencing the REAL version of the band because, as we all know, the Grateful Dead excelled on stage in a live setting.  They have a TON of live albums available for purchase; most of which will never see the light of day in my CD player.  I just don't see the need to devote all my time and energy to the Grateful Dead by purchasing a bucketload of their live albums when there's literally hundreds of other artists and guitar players more deserving of my time.      I believe Blues for Allah is a really good album.  It's no American Beauty of course, but I don't really care about that.  There's a reason Blues for Allah is one of the more popular studio albums by the Grateful Dead- the songwriting and the guitar solos are -contrary to my negative review- a little better than usual this time around, so I recommend a purchase.

0316017930|4.0|An outlier is something that appears to deviate markedly from other members of the sample in which it occurs.  The book Outliers studies marked deviations from mainstream in areas from successful people, unusually high airline crash rates, genius IQ levels, and more.    In regards to the subject of success, the author writes that \\"society's notion is that the best and the brightest who effortlessly rise to the top is much too simplistic.\\"  In the story about the Canadian hockey players who were predominately born in Jan, Feb, or March, sociologist Robert Merton calls this phenomenon the \\"Matthew Effect\\" after the New Testament verse in the gospel of Matthew:  \\"For unto everyone that hath shall be given, and he shall have abundance.  But from him that hath not shall be taken away even that which he hath.\\"  Success breeds success.  The author writes: \\"If you make a decision about who is good and who is not good at an early age; if you separate the \\"talented\\" from the \\"untalented\\"; and if you provide the \\"talented\\" with a superior experience, then you're going to end up giving a huge advantage to that small group of people born closest to the cutoff date.\\"  In relation to this, we see how the family support system is so crucial to a child's future success.  Child actors and actresses are a great example.  How many young actors and actresses have a passion and talent for acting, but don't have support from family and friends, nor the opportunities to practice and let their talent shine or be noticed.  Conversely, how many young and successful actors and actresses became a success because of a \\"superior experience:\\" a supportive home and parents who challenged their son or daughter to practice their passion, enrolled them in acting classes, acting clubs, and the like, and helped foster their passion and ambition for acting.  It's not limited to acting, but so many other career examples that show that a child's upbringing and support system (or lack thereof) is without adoubt a very large contributing factor to the child's future success.     In chapter two, entitled The Ten-Thousand Hour Rule, it's easy to think that those individuals who display mastery over a skill were automatically destined to be a success because they were born with a god-given talent.  However, the truth is that to master anything, talent alone will not get you there.  One must have an incredible drive and discipline to practice and work very hard to hone that skill.  The author uses gifted musicians as an example, and writes:  \\"...research suggests that once a musician has enough ability to get into a top music school, the thing that distinguishes one performer from another is how hard he or she works.  That's it.  And what's more, the people at the very top don't work just harder or even much harder than anyone everyone else.  They work much, much harder.\\"  The author cites the results of a study which concludes from research that the \\"magic number\\" for true expertise is 10,000 hours of practice.  Broken down into sizable chunks, this equates to three hours a day, 365 days a year, for 9.1 years!  A story was told that in the 90's, Larry Bird was hired by Sprite to be part of a TV commercial.  Part of the act was for Bird to shoot a shot from about 12 feet out and intentionally miss.  It took Bird seven attempts before he finally missed the shot!  It's no argument that Bird was one of the greatest basketball players ever to play the game.  But Larry Bird wasn't born with a skill that guaranteed him as spot in the NBA.  Larry Bird's work ethic and hours of practice are what got him there. Larry Bird's KC Jones, relayed a story that after winning his first championship with the Celtics in the 80's, Bird was in the gym the next morning, practicing for next season!  Larry Bird knew that true mastery over the game of basketball required a lot of practice and hard work.      In chapter seven, entitled The Ethnic Theory of Plane Crashes, the discussion on \\"mitigated speech\\" brought to mind what can happen at many companies when people in power are given this type speech by their subordinates.  Mitigated speech is defined as any attempt to downplay or sugarcoat the meaning of what is being said, such as when one is being deferential to authority.  The author uses the example of a captain and crew on an airplane.  Successful flying involves teamwork and effective communication between the captain, co-pilot and others.  Mitigated speech to the captain from the co-pilot and crew can be detrimental to the safety of the flight and everyone on board.  The captain and crew need direct, unmitigated communication from one another to be successful.  When business and marketing plans demonstrate obviously poor decisions, one must wonder if mitigated speech contributed to such bad decisions.  One example of a poor business and marketing decision is New Coke.  It was a disaster! Why did Coke replace the number one soda on the market, loved by millions of people around the globe, with a new flavor and call it \\"New Coke\\".  As the saying goes, \\"If it ain't broke, don't fix it.\\"  This might lead one to wonder how many at Coke were using mitigated speech with their superiors before New Coke was launched, rather than coming out and saying \\"This idea is bad! Stick with what we have.\\"  One wonders if the decision-maker(s) behind the production and launch of New Coke were being paid lip service by their subordinates who were afraid to express disagreement.      It was intriguing to read the way in which Koreans communicate with one another.  The author shows how Korea, like many Asian countries, is receiver oriented in its communication.  Meaning it is the listener's responsibility the make sense of what is being said.  Here is an example the author uses:    Kwacang: It's cold and I'm hungry  (Meaning: Why don't you buy a drink or something to eat?)  Mr. Kim:  How about having a glass of liquor?  (Meaning:  I will buy liquor for you.)  Kwacang:  It's okay. Don't bother.  (Meaning:  I will accept your offer if you repeat it.)  Mr. Kim: You must be hungry. How about going out?  (Meaning: I insist upon treating you.)  Kwacang:  Shall I do so?  (Meaning: I accept.)     To a westerner reading this two-way communication probably appears very indirect in comparison to the manner of speaking in the west. Western culture is very direct and transmitter oriented.  It is the sender's responsibility to make sense of what is being said, not the listeners.   It would seem as though the two Koreans are \\"beating around the bush\\" and not getting to the point. However, the beauty in the exchange above is the polite nature in which it is delivered, leaving no room for insensitivity or indifference.  It's easy to understand how this type of communication between a co-pilot and captain, in a culture of high respect and regard for authority like Korea, could contribute to an outlier degree of flight crashes by the Korean airlines, cited in chapter seven.     To conclude, the book Outliers showed how skewed results in such areas as success, honor, flight safety and more, were heavily influenced and formed by such things as family support, culture, race, and upbringing.  I enjoyed reading the results of studies, and discovering why the particular Outliers existed. I was reminded that things are not always what they appear to be on the surface.
